"382"	"it"	"is"	" It may be that users with the whole world at their fingertips will not choose to dally with their neighbors. After all, Palo Alto, for all that it may be crowded with Silicon Valley grandees, is only a small island in the net. But for its residents, it is still the place outside the door. And that will always count for something. </P> </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19940708.0056 type=multi > <HEADLINE> CAPSULE REVIEWS OF CURRENT FILMS </HEADLINE> <DATELINE>  (BC-MINI-REVIEWS-(5takes)-COX) </DATELINE> <TEXT> These brief reviews of current movies are written by Cox News Service movie critic Eleanor Ringel. They move weekly to clients of the New York Times News Service.   G: General audiences. Nothing that would offend parents if viewed by children.   PG: Parental guidance suggested. May contain some material parents consider inappropriate for young children.   PG-13: Parents strongly cautioned. Some material may be inappropriate for preteens.   R: Under 17 requires accompanying parent or adult guardian. Parents are urged to learn more about the film before taking children.   NC-17: No one under 17 admitted. Replaces the X rating.   NR: Not rated.   . . STILL PLAYING   Above the Rim R   A perfectly commendable above-average after-school special, except for one problem: It thinks it's a real movie, aimed, perhaps, at the ``New Jack City'' crowd. Duane Martin (a Knicks prospect-turned-actor) stars as a high school hotshot who sees a college athletic scholarship as his ticket out of Harlem and into the NBA.   Ace Ventura: Pet Detective PG-13   Jim Carrey, the ``In Living Color'' regular plays an animal-loving goofball who specializes in missing pets. The script, alas, is the sort of appalling mishmash often used to launch cross-over stand-up comics. It's full of needless T-and-A jokes and homophobic humor that make it inappropriate for the younger teens who'd otherwise get the biggest kick out of the star's antics.   Baby's Day Out PG   A very long day indeed, for Baby and for the audience. This John Hughes-ian spin on ``The Ransom of Red Chief'' is best recommended to die-hard slapstick fans who've always wanted to see a remake of ``Three Men and a Baby,'' starring the Three Stooges. Posing as baby photographers, three bumbling crooks (Joe Mantegna, Joe Pantoliano, Brian Haley) kidnap 9-month-old Bennington August Cottwell IV, a.k.a., Baby Bink. They quickly learn that grabbing a curious tot is a lot easier than holding onto him.   Bad Girls R   Madeleine Stowe, Andie MacDowell, Drew Barrymore and Mary Stuart Masterson are working girls out West, on the run to Oregon, after Stowe plugs a murderously unruly client. While it's nice to see, finally, that even cowgirls get to ride off into the sunset, overall, ``Bad Girls'' just wants to have fun. You will, too.   Bitter Moon R   One loony movie. Internationally known naughty-boy Roman Polanski takes us on a luxury cruise with two couples. Nigel and Fiona (Hugh Grant and Kristin Scott Thomas) are stiff-upper-lip Brits trying to rekindle their marriage. Wheelchair-bound Oscar and his voluptuous wife, Mimi (Peter Coyote and Emmanuelle Seigner) are . . . well, what they are is pretty much the point of this hilariously preposterous tale of sexual obsession.   Blink R   Never mind the stink-bomb title. This is a sublimely entertaining romantic thriller with a pair of stars (Madeleine Stowe and Aidan Quinn) who are that rare thing: startlingly beautiful actors who can also act. Quinn is a Chicago detective with a messy murder on his hands. Luckily, he's got an eyewitness . . . of sorts. Stowe may have seen the killer leave the scene. The operative word is may; thanks to a recent corneal transplant, she's still not sure what she sees and what she thinks she sees.   Blown Away R   This so-so thriller won't blow anyone away. Jeff Bridges is a steely nerved, magic-fingered veteran of the Boston Bomb Squad; Tommy Lee Jones is a psycho who can ``make a bomb out of Bisquick.'' Because of a shared shady past, Jones becomes obsessed with Bridges and starts blowing away everyone near and dear to him.  </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19940708.0057 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-MINI-REVIEWS-(2ndtake)-COX) </DATELINE> <TEXT> City Slickers II -- The Legend of Curly's Gold PG-13   Not as feel-good slick as its hugely popular predecessor, but funnier and far loopier in its own shaggy-dog way. Billy Crystal co-wrote, produced and stars in this sequel that sends him out West in search of buried gold. The movie is a half-hour too long, but at least Crystal hasn't pulled a ``Rocky,'' i.e., re-made the exact same movie and affixed a Roman numeral to it.   The Cowboy Way PG-13   The Woody Way. This action-comedy is going to catapult Woody Harrelson waaaaay up the ranks of Hollywood leading men. His self-assuredness -- make that, cocksuredness -- is the most interesting aspect of this otherwise sadly synthetic fish-out-of-water film. Harrelson and Kiefer Sutherland play rodeo champs from New Mexico who brave the arcane terrors of New York City to rescue a Cuban friend and his daughter.   The Crow R   The true- life tragedy hanging over this action film -- a misfired prop gun that killed star Brandon Lee on the set -- is a lot creepier than anything in the movie itself. Still, it's more than a morbid curiosity, thanks to the young star himself; this doomed son of the late Bruce Lee inherited more from his famous dad than bad karma. Lee plays a dead rock musician who rises from the grave to avenge the murders of himself and his fiancee.   D2: The Mighty Ducks PG   The mightiest thing about the first ``Mighty Ducks'' was its box-office clout -- far more than anyone expected from a hackneyed rehash of ``The Bad News Bears,'' with an almost-has-been Emilio Estevez as its star. This lame sequel simply repeats the same underdog plot.   The Endless Summer II PG   It's been a long time between waves for filmmaker Bruce Brown who, in 1964, parlayed a $50,000 paean to the zen of surfing into a $30 million hit. The surf's still up in this sequel of sorts as Brown focuses his camera on impish Robert ``Wingnut'' Weaver and towheaded Pat O'Connell. These two embark on a globe- trotting odyssey from Costa Rica to Java, in search of the perfect wave. When the movie stays in the water, it is, to coin a phrase, awesome. But on dry land, the movie is all wet.   Even Cowgirls Get the Blues R   Even talented directors make mistakes. And Gus Van Sant (``My Own Private Idaho'') has made a doozy with this abysmal adaptation of Tom Robbins's 1976 novel. The movie focuses on Sissy Hankshaw (a blank Uma Thurman), a former model who's also the best hitchhiker in the nation. Her secret: thumbs the size of kumquats.   Fear of a Black Hat R   It's a black thing and everyone will understand. That's how very funny, how very accessible this hilarious spoof is. No matter how rap- savvy you are or aren't (you can't tell Kris Kross from Salt-N-Pepa or you know every track on ``Fear of a Black Planet''), you'll be knocked out by this rude, crude, no-holds-barred satire. Filmmaker Rusty Cundieff does for rap what ``Spinal Tap'' did for heavy metal; he skewers it with a mix of affection, wit and insight.   The Flintstones PG   Hits rock bottom. The early buzz around Hollywood on this live-action version of the '60s animated TV series was that it ``skewed young.'' Translation: It's even stupider than they meant it to be. The plot, which somehow needed the talents of 35 writers, follows Fred's (John Goodman) precipitous ``Hudsucker Proxy''-ish rise from quarry worker to vice president of Slate and Co. when a villainous exec (Kyle MacLachlan) and his shapely assistant (Halle Berry) need a fall guy for their greedy schemes.   Forrest Gump PG-13   Tom Hanks is clueless in America in Robert Zemeckis's half-baked fable about an idiot-savant of sorts, skimming through the last half of the 20th century and colliding, ``Zelig''-like, with history. Forrest, who has the IQ of a kumquat, nevertheless manages to play football for Bear Bryant, become a hero in Vietnam, visit with presidents, play pingpong in Mao's China, etc. It's Hollywood's idea of an offbeat yet profound fable about the meaning of life; alas, it comes off like an Oliver Stone movie starring Gomer Pyle. Zemeckis creates an effective and often hilarious running gag in Forrest's constant run-ins with destiny -- whether teaching Elvis his swivel-hipped shimmy or calling security on a certain fateful night at the Watergate Hotel. But the movie is a one-trick pony, a superb technological gimmick/cultural cosmic joke tied to a TV-movie plot. Far more impressive than Zemeckis's techno- sleight-of-hand is the steadying integrity of the Hanks portrayal. His Southern- fried Holy Fool brings the movie focus, giving it a heart to make up for its lack of brains.   Four Weddings and a Funeral R   Hugh Grant is a charming British bachelor -- the always-a-groomsman-never-the-groom type -- who meets an equally charming American (Andie MacDowell) at the first of the film's nuptials. His not-quite- pursuit, not-quite-not-pursuit of her is the main plot-thread of this stylish froth. However, most of the film is about the delicate balance of emotion and humor that's inherent in such life-changing social rituals. This dapper valentine of a movie delivers even more than the title promises: four weddings, a funeral and a happy ending.  </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19940708.0058 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-MINI-REVIEWS-(3rdtake)-COX) </DATELINE> <TEXT> Getting Even With Dad PG   The Mac is back -- sans ballet slippers and doing what he does best. Namely, acting out every kid's fantasy of outwitting every available adult and getting away with it. A ponytailed Ted Danson plays an ex-con who's been tempted into one last job -- stealing a cache of rare coins. However, he's suddenly saddled with his estranged son (Macaulay Culkin) who soon figures out what Danson is up to and has his own plan for the purloined coins, i.e., using them as blackmail to make his dad behave like a dad for a week.   Go Fish R   Director/writer Rose Troche has made one of the best relationship movies of the year; it just happens to be about same-sex relationships. Set in a vibrant lesbian community in Chicago, this romantic comedy is a girl-meets-girl story whose gender orientation may be specific and, for most mainstream audiences, exotic. However, the film's ruefully on-target observations about the perils of the dating game are pretty universal.   I Love Trouble PG   I love Julia Roberts and I love Nick Nolte but I didn't love their movie, no matter how hard I tried. Chances are, you won't either. The two play rival reporters working the same story. The movie just gets thinner and thinner until there's nothing to hold your attention except the leads. Luckily, that's what Nolte and Roberts do best: he's grizzled and adorable; she's incandescent and adorable. But their movie gives them nothing but trouble.   The Inkwell R   Where is Charles Burnett when we need him? This extremely promising material -- about family clashes, summer crushes and more during a 1976 beach vacation -- has been directed by Matty Rich (``Straight Out of Brooklyn'') who simply doesn't yet have the maturity to explore the story's wonderful array of characters and contradictions.   Jurassic Park PG-13   Steven Spielberg's spectacular dinosaur extravaganza has raptors and brachiosaurus and more oh-my! than any movie in recent memory.   Like Water for Chocolate R   Set in the early 20th century on a sprawling ranch near the U.S. border, the movie is about Tita (Lumi Cavazos) who's literally born in a kitchen and then tied to her tyrannical mother's apron strings by a family tradition that decrees the youngest daughter must never marry (instead, she must devote her life to taking care of mom). Relegated to the role of family cook while her sister marries her beloved (Marco Leonardi), Tita becomes something of a kitchen magician, i.e., whatever she's feeling is channelled into whatever she's cooking.   The Lion King G   A royal entertainment, fit for kids and kings. This classy, beautifully drawn cartoon effortlessly takes its place in the superb cycle of Disney neo- classics.The lion cub Simba (voice by Jonathan Taylor Thomas, then Matthew Broderick) must reclaim his throne after his evil uncle, Scar (Jeremy Irons doing Claus Von Bulow with a mane), connives to have Simba's father, King Mufasa (James Earl Jones), killed.   Little Big League PG   A baseball-mad 12-year-old (Luke Edwards) becomes the new owner/manager of the Minnesota Twins when his gramps (Jason Robards) leaves him the team in his will. The boy helps the Twins discover their winning ways by reclaiming their inner child. The movie is innocuous enough, if you don't mind how brazenly it rips off the far better ``Rookie of the Year.''   Little Buddha PG   You won't find nirvana in this visually ravishing, dramatically inept epic about Buddhism. The picture is an unwieldy hybrid of two stories. In the past, Prince Siddhartha (Keanu Reeves) leaves his protected palace to learn about suffering and death. In the present, an aged Tibetan monk goes to Seattle to see if a boy named Jesse is the reincarnation of his former master.   Maverick PG-13   In the parlance of its poker-playing hero, this dumb- fun western draws a winning hand. Not a royal flush, perhaps, but a solid three- of-a-kind -- the three being Mel Gibson, Jodie Foster and James Garner, who glide smoothly over the movie's rougher spots.   Monkey Trouble PG   An innocuously enjoyable kids' film with some nice things to say about responsibility and learning to get along with stepfamilies. Thora Birch (``Paradise'') stars as an animal-mad 9-year-old who really wants a pet. Trouble is, her new stepdad is really allergic to fur. So when an adorable Capucin monkey literally drops out into her arms out of nowhere, she's ecstatic -- and very careful to keep him hidden. What she doesn't know is that her new pet in on the lam from his brutal owner (Harvey Keitel) who trained him as a pickpocket par excellence.  </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19940708.0059 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-MINI-REVIEWS-(4thtake)-COX) </DATELINE> <TEXT> Naked Gun 331/3 PG-13   More comic overkill from the ``Naked Gun'' hit factory. Leslie Nielsen is back as Lt. Frank Drebin, the best (worst?) thing to happen to law enforcement since Inspector Clousseau. Newly retired from the squad, he's called back into duty to foil a terrorist plot being hatched by Fred Ward.   The Paper R   OK, so it's about as accurate about life at a newspaper as the ``Naked Gun'' movies are about police work. That said, this is still an awfully entertaining picture, one that captures the cynicism, the sick humor, the deadline pressure and the sheer adrenaline-rush love of putting out a daily.   Philadelphia PG-13   Tom Hanks gives an Oscar- winning performance as a fast-track lawyer who sues his former firm after being fired for incompetence (hmmm, and they just gave him a promotion). Convinced he was let go because he was HIV-positive, Hanks wants his day in court to prove it.   The Princess and the Goblin G   This is a perfectly pleasant animated feature based on a popular Victorian children's novel by Scotsman George MacDonald. Princess Irene ventures out of her protected castle one day and discovers there are goblins out there. Literally. Worse, they're plotting to take over her father's kingdom. The movie's still-timely message -- find the magic inside yourself -- is imparted by Irene's ghostly great-great grandmother who can make roses appear out of thin air.   Schindler's List R   Steven Spielberg's hauntingly powerful, must-see Holocaust epic. This is the true story of an unlikely saint named Oskar Schindler (Liam Neeson.) who during World War II saved more than 1,100 Jews from the Nazi ovens.   Serial Mom R   How nice to report that John Waters is still alive and well (in his own peculiar fashion) and living in Baltimore. His movie is a frisky suburban farce about a homicidal happy homemaker (Kathleen Turner) who just has to express herself when she sees someone who doesn't recycle or refuses to rewind their rental video.   The Shadow PG-13   It's a shame ``The Shadow'' came along after ``Batman'' and ``Dick Tracy.'' It borrows the best elements of both -- ``Batman's'' brooding cityscapes and eerie atmosphere, combined with ``Tracy's'' lighter touch and wittier art design. But the inevitable comparisons make Russell Mulcahy's film seem redundant even though Alec Baldwin does a sterling job as Lamont Cranston, the mysterious, slouch-brimmed figure from '30s radio and pulp fiction.   Sirens R   Poised somewhere between soft- core porn and sex farce, this droll examination of the ongoing clash between status-quo conservatism and artistic freedom isn't always successful. But it has its moments. In '30s Australia, a proper minister and his wife (Hugh Grant and Tara Fitzgerald) are sent to a painter's (Sam Neill) retreat. Their mission: to persuade Neill to replace his ``vulgar'' (so says the bishop) paintings in a Sydney museum with something more suitable.   The Snapper R   A rowdy warmth and exhuberant high-spirits permeate every frame of Stephen Frears' terrific little Irish comedy. Sharing the same author (novelist Roddy Dyoe) and working-class Dublin setting as ``The Commitments,'' the movie centers on the Curley family in which 20-year-old Sharon (Tina Kellegher) has a bit of a surprise for her parents (Colm Meaney and Ruth McCabe). She's pregnant -- and she's not saying who the father is.   Speed R The perfect summer-movie rush. ``Speed'' hits the ground running and never lets up. The gimmick is simple: A wacko (Dennis Hopper) has put a bomb on a city bus. When the bus hits 50 mph, the bomb goes on; if it slows below 50, the bomb goes off. And so the chase is on -- with a busload of terrified passengers, a hurt driver, a spunky civilian at the wheel (the winning Sandra Bullock) and one very determined cop (Keanu Reeves) in pursuit.   Sugar Hill R   There's very little that's sweet in this mean-streets melodrama about two brothers who become successful Harlem drug lords. Wesley Snipes is the good son, who brings his derelict-junkie dad (Clarence Williams III) chicken soup and is increasingly conscience-stricken about how he earns a living. Michael Wright is the prodigal son -- hotheaded and hooked on his own poison. Not quite art-house solid, not-quite action- flick slick, ``Sugar Hill'' is something of a hybrid. But it's exceedingly well- crafted, expertly performed and certainly worth a look.  </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19940708.0060 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-MINI-REVIEWS-(5thtake)-COX) </DATELINE> <TEXT> Surviving the Game R   Ernest Dickerson, best known as Spike Lee's cinematographer, proves he's far more than just the man behind the Spike with this story about wealthy, debauched hunters who pay billions to hunt a human prey. A homeless man (Ice-T, exuding a quiet cool) is the innocent, smoothly recruited by a seemingly caring social worker and sent running for his life through the gorgeous northwest wilderness.   3 Ninjas Kick Back PG   Pure formula, but pure fun. Like its hugely successful predecessor, this sequel is an unpretentiously entertaining kids' film -- a slow starter that just gets better and better as it goes along. This time, our three brothers (Sean Fox, Max Elliott Slade, Evan Bonifant) accompany their grandpa (Victor Wong) to Japan where they stumble into a ``Raiders''-like adventure involving an ancient dagger, a secret treasure cave and three ``jerko metalheads.''   When a Man Loves a Woman R   A movie that proves Meg Ryan looks better with a bad hangover than most of us looked at our senior prom. She and Andy Garcia play a seemingly perfect couple. There's just one little problem: Ryan drinks like a fish. However, the movie is less about her alcoholism than the effect it has on her family.   White R   Wonderful. The second film in Polish director Krystof Kieslowski's trilogy inspired by the French tricolor flag and motto (liberty, equality, fraternity) couldn't be more different from its melancholy, mystic predecessor, ``Blue.'' An expatriot Polish hairdresser (Zibgniew Zamachowski), returns home (in a hilarious ordeal) from Paris when his luscious wife (Julie Delpy) divorces him. After losing everything -- she accuses him of impotence, takes all his money and sets the French police after him -- he enjoys an incredible reversal of fortune in the economic hurly-burly of post-Communist Warsaw and hatches a bizarre scheme to win Delpy back.   Widows' Peak PG   Widows peek a lot in this winning Irish comedy. They also snoop, meddle, gossip, sneak around and generally engage in every possible manifestation of the time-honored art of exercising female power in a close-knit society (a.k.a., the Nosy Neighbor Network). Set in 1920s Ireland, the title refers to a hill above a small village that's entirely populated by well- heeled widows. The movie stars Joan Plowright, Mia Farrow and Natasha Richardson.   With Honors PG-13   Joe Pesci is part Ali MacGraw in ``Love Story,'' part Dustin Hoffman as Ratso Rizzo in this well-intentioned but decidedly unwieldy story of a homeless man (Pesci) adopted by a Harvard senior (Brendan Fraser) and his housemates (Moira Kelly, Patrick Dempsey and Josh Hamilton).   Wolf R   A slickly entertaining guilty pleasure. Jack Nicholson, Michelle Pfeiffer and Mike Nichols have lavished their A-list talent on a '90s version of an old Lon Chaney Jr. werewolf movie.   Wyatt Earp PG-13   Directed by Lawrence Kasdan and starring Kevin Costner as the famed lawman, this is a really good movie that's a half hour too long. The film covers a lot of territory as it follows Wyatt from his Kansas boyhood to his adventures out West, ricocheting from Wichita to Dodge and finally to Tombstone and 30 seconds of immortality at the O.K. Corral. .  </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19940708.0061 type=story > <HEADLINE> FOOD: SEPTUAGENARIAN SALAD BOWLS OVER A NEW GENERATION WITH ITS AGELESS APPEAL </HEADLINE> <DATELINE>  (BC-CAESAR-SALADS-COX) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_199407.gz"

They move weekly to clients of the New York Times News Service.   G: General audiences. Nothing that would offend parents if viewed by children.   PG: Parental guidance suggested. May contain some material parents consider inappropriate for young children.   PG-13: Parents strongly cautioned. Some material may be inappropriate for preteens.   R: Under 17 requires accompanying parent or adult guardian. Parents are urged to learn more about the film before taking children.   NC-17: No one under 17 admitted. Replaces the X rating.   NR: Not rated.   Bad Company R   This designer noir would like to take its place alongside such dark gems as ``Prizzi's Honor,'' ``The Grifters'' and ``The Last Seduction.'' But it's a poseur. As in his Goldie Hawn vehicle ``Deceived'' (1991), director Damian Harriss wamps the movie in portentous nocturnal shadows and slows things to a crawl just when the flick needs a good jolt. Laurence Fishburne and Ellen Barkin star. Rated R for strong sexuality, violence and language. -- Steve Murray   Before Sunrise R   Julie Delpy and Ethan Hawke play strangers on a train who become lov ers in Vienna, Austria, when she accepts his impetuous invitation to join him for an all-night waltz around the city. It sounds terribly romantic and, at times, it is, but their relationship -- with the audience and each other -- never grows. Director Richard Linklater, whose ``Slacker'' and ``Dazed and Confused'' were such brilliant distillations of a certain generation, has given us little more than a one-night-stand to remember.   Billy Madison PG- 13   ``Saturday Night Live's'' Adam Sandler brings his ultrasophisticated brand of dry humor (insert armpit noise here) to this comedy about a lovable slacker who must repeat all 12 grades of school in order to secure a place in his millionaire father's will. Not prescreened for critics. -- Doug Hamilton   Blue NR   Krzysztof Kieslowski's coolly stunning mood piece stars Juliette Binoche (``Damage'') as a young widow. After her husband -- a world-famous composer -- and daughter are killed in a car crash, Binoche cuts herself off from family, friends, lovers and, most crucially, any semblance of the passionate, creative person she was before the tragedy (it's hinted she wrote her husband's music). Binoche brings a lovely stillness to her role, leaving the emotionalism to director Kieslowski, whose lush soundtrack and immaculate images have a hypnotic pull.   Boys on the Side R   Whoopi Goldberg, Drew Barrymore and Mary-Louise Parker are smack in the middle of a highly entertaining New Age weepie. Though the on-the-road ambience suggests ``Thelma &AMP; Louise'' (Whoopi even makes a crack about ``not driving off a cliff with you two!''), this movie's true model is ``Terms of Endearment.'' Only, these are definitely '90s terms, with some definitely '90s ideas of alternative families and a healthy smattering of such now-familiar talk-show fodder as lesbianism, unwed moms and AIDS. Goldberg is an acid- tongued Manhattan club singer who needs to get out of town. So she answers a ride-share ad posted by Parker, a terminally perky real estate agent who enjoys Carpenters sing-alongs. The pair then pick up Goldberg's old pal Barrymore, rescuing her from an abusive boyfriend. Thanks to an illness and a pregnancy, their road trip ends in Tucson, Ariz., where they set up an unorthodox household much like the eccentric extended family in ``Bagdad Cafe.'' Director Herbert Ross isn't exactly the cutting-edge type, but he knows how to handle actors and he's not afraid to take the material on its own terms -- as a hip tear-jerker, as agile as it is blubbery. This isn't a profound film or even a risky one, but it's solid through and through. Rated R for language and adult themes.   Demon Knight R   A tongue-in-cheek gorefest that's not quite cheeky enough, spun off the ``Tales From the Crypt'' TV series. Billy Zane stars. Rated R for violence and language. -- Steve Murray  </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950210.0074 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-MINI-REVIEWS-(2ndtake)-COX) </DATELINE> <TEXT> Disclosure R   Michael Crichton's provocative best seller about a female boss (Demi Moore) who manhandles -- so to speak -- a male underling (Michael Douglas) and then charges him with sexual harassment isn't a bad set-up for a movie. But, thanks to director Barry Levinson's ham-fisted approach, the material has been reduced to the level of ``Basic Instinct''/``Fatal Attraction'' trash. Rated R for sex and language.   Dumb and Dumber PG-13   The final entry in Jim Carrey's Doofus Trilogy '94, after mega-hits ``Ace Ventura: Pet Detective'' and ``The Mask.'' Easily the most lowbrow ridiculous of the three, ``Dumb'' is barely a movie at all -- it's more like a visual volume of ``Truly Tasteless Jokes'' occasionally interrupted by the most anemic suspense plot imaginable. It's also a riot. Rated PG-13 for language and adult situations. -- Doug Hamilton   Far From Home:   The Adventures   of Yellow Dog PG   Set in the spectacular forests of British Columbia, this pleasant family picture is a straightforward tale of a boy and his dog. Only this boy (Jesse Bradford from ``King of the Hill'') and this dog -- a soulful blond lab named Dakotah -- are lost together in the Canadian wilderness. Rated PG for mild violence.   Higher Learning R   John Singleton's third film proves that trouble is just as preval ent outside the hood in ``Higher Learning.'' Set on a fictional, racially mixed university campus, Malik Williams (Omar Epps), Kristen Connor (Kristy Swanson) and Remy (Michael Rapaport) face race and social relations at their worst, all in the course of one year. When the final bell rings, ``Higher Learning'' is as emotionally taxing as tonight's blood-drenched evening newscast. Both are stirring, true. But one's free. Rated R for offensive language, nudity and violence. -- Sonia Murray   Houseguest PG   In this latest prefab comedy from the Disney assembly line, comic Sinbad stars as Kevin, a big Pittsburgh dreamer wanted by loan sharks. He shakes them by posing as Derek, a long unseen friend of businessman Gary (Phil Hartman), who's expecting him for a weekend visit in his plush suburban home. The film has fun near the start, as Kevin/Derek tries to piece together clues about his alter ego's occupation, but every time the movie starts to rev up some wild energy, the tired formula slows everything down. Basically this is the story of a role-model homeboy who moves into the Laura Ashley suburbs, loosens up Hartman's uptight WASP character, and solves the family's domestic problems. He's the street-smart Barney. -- Steve Murray   Immortal Beloved R   As visually lush as it is musically glorious, this clever conjecture on Beethoven's love life emphasizes entertainment, not accuracy. Gary Oldman stars. -- Steve Murray   In the Mouth of Madness R   A rip-roaring horror flick from John Carpenter. Sam Neill plays a private investigator hired to find a missing best-selling horror writer named Sutter Cane (Jurgen Prochnow). Worse, as far as his publisher, Charlton Heston, is concerned, he's disappeared before finishing his next book. Neill smells a PR scam, especially when he hears rumors about Cane's books driving some readers mad. But he agrees to go with Cane's editor (Julie Carmen) to New England in search of Cane. What they find . . . well, that would be telling. Let's just say that Carpenter, who practically reinvented the horror film with ``Halloween,'' still knows his way around scary stuff -- from dead-eyed children to ax-wielding maniacs. The niftiest thing about the film is the way its feverish tone evokes cult horror writer H.P. Lovecraft.   Interview With the Vampire R   As richly textured as a dark brocade, threaded with existential dread and drenched in plasma, Neil Jordan's film will disturb many viewers. It's filled with snapping necks, slit throats, rats and wrists draining crimson into goblets, and other bloody bits of necro-fun. But it's likely to thrill Anne Rice fans and seduce those unfamiliar with her books. Starring Brad Pitt and Tom Cruise. Rated R for violence, gore, nudity and sexuality. -- Steve Murray   I.Q. PG-13   Directed by Fred Schepisi (``Roxanne''), this comedy about the romance between hearty mechanic Ed (Tim Robbins) and heady mathematician Catherine (Meg Ryan) zips by with sparkle. It's not a 4.0, but ``I.Q.'' scores high enough to pass. Rated PG for mild language and adult themes. -- Steve Murray   The Jerky Boys R   With their phone-crank outrages, the Jerky Boys personify the urban id unleashed. Ranting about hemorrhoids and all things anal, they shatter the daily grind with scatological rudeness. Bless 'em. Too bad they're being packaged in this pathetic showcase, a tired, Disneyfied chase comedy involving the mob and crooked cops. The lame plot line might have worked 30 years ago as a crossover vehicle for, say, the Monkees. But it deflates the Boys' furious nonsense. And tames the previously faceless Johnny Brennan and Kamal Ahmed into a couple of paunchy, bland ``lowlifes from Queens,'' who don't exactly exude star charisma. The guys' phone riffs -- especially the unstoppably foulmouthed Brennan's -- are still potent for their sheer gleeful shockmanship and political incorrectness (especially Brennan's highly offensive ``gay'' voice). But the movie they're stuck in is strictly Prince Albert in a Can. Rated R for strong language. -- Steve Murray  </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950210.0075 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-MINI-REVIEWS-(3rdtake)-COX) </DATELINE> <TEXT> The Jungle Book PG   For kids, there's a bunch of cool animals. For adults, there's Jason Scott Lee in a loincloth. But for Rudyard Kipling fans, there's not much. It's like director Stephen Sommers' ``Adventures of Huck Finn'' last year -- another example of great source material that lacked an animating spark. -- Steve Murray   The Last Seduction R   Probably the sleeper of the year. In the tradition of ``Double Indemnity'' and ``Body Heat,'' director John Dahl (``Red Rock West'') pairs a smart, sexy, no-good dame with a hunky sucker who's not half as smart as he thinks he is. Linda Fiorentino is a one-woman wrecking crew as a tough cookie with a lotta leg and even more nerve who easily gets nice-guy Peter Berg in her thrall. Rated R for sex, violence, language, nudity and adult themes.   Legends of the Fall R   Think of the very best ``Bonanza'' episode you ever saw; then cast it with the likes of Anthony Hopkins, Brad Pitt and Aidan Quinn; finally add in the sibling rivalry/father-always-liked-you-best themes of ``East of Eden.'' Yummy. Rated R for sex, nudity and violence.   The Lion King G   Disney's feature-length cartoon is royal entertainment. Sort of ``Bambi'' meets ``Hamlet'' on the Serengeti, it roars onto the screen with vivid characters and expert animation. Featuring the voices of James Earl Jones, Jeremy Irons and Matthew Broderick. Rated G, but there is a death that may disturb young children.   Little Women PG   Director Gillian Armstrong (``My Brilliant Career'') and her solid cast, including Winona Ryder and Susan Sarandon, have imbued the third screen version of Louisa May Alcott's classic with considerable care and craftsmanship -- every scene looks like a Currier and Ives print. Rated PG for mild adult themes.   The Madness   of King George Not rated   The year is 1788. King George III (Nigel Hawthorne) already has lost the Colonies. Now he's losing his mind. Based on Alan Bennett's much- praised play and artfully transferred from the stage by Nicholas Hytner, this historical whimsy recalls ``The Lion in Winter'' or ``The Ruling Class.'' But Hawthorne's extraordinary portrayal gives ``Madness'' its own unforgettable voice. He's definitely Oscar-worthy.   The Mask PG-13   Forget the superhero plot of this live-action cartoon and focus on the Looney-Tunes special effects, the immensely likable Jim Carrey and Milo the mighty Jack Russell terrier. Directed by Chuck Russell. Rated   PG-13 for mild violence.   Miami Rhapsody PG-13   Percolating to a mix of heady one-liners and romantic angst, writer- director David Frankel's disarmingly witty comedy examines the pitfalls of love (and lust) in the '90s. Sarah Jessica Parker is the film's invaluable motor, equally adept at spewing Mensa-level quips and dipping into slapstick. Frankel peppers his script with so many zingers, you want to curb your laughter so as not to miss the next one. Rated PG-13 for language and adult themes. -- Steve Murray   Murder in the First R   The more-or-less true story of a history-making case. In 1941, after spending years in solitary confinement for attempted escape (the usual maximum was 19 days), Alcatraz inmate Henri Young (Kevin Bacon) killed the convict who'd ratted on him in front of 200 witnesses. An ambitious young attorney (Christian Slater) took the case and decided to put the system on trial. This is good material and the actors give it their best. Alas, director Marc Rocco is his own -- and their -- worst enemy. His distinctively active camera has a certain panache, but it soon becomes an irritating affectation that detracts from the performances and the story. Rated R for violence, nudity, language and sex.   Nell PG-13   A gentle miracle of a movie with an Oscar-worthy performance by Jodie Foster. She plays the title character: a feral backwoods North Carolina woman who's been raised in almost total isolation from the modern world (No phone, no TV, etc.). Liam Neeson and Natasha Richardson are rival doctors who become fascinated by her. Rated PG-13 for nudity.   Nobody's Fool R   Even when director Robert Benton grabs for the heartstrings, star Paul Newman tempers the sentiment and gives a career-capping performance as a father at the crossroads. Rated R for language and nudity. -- Steve Murray   Pulp Fiction R   Pop goes the pulp in this smashing sophomore effort by Quentin ``Reservoir Dogs'' Tarantino. Winner of the Golden Palm at Cannes and subject to Oscar-time whispers, the picture is a gleefully lurid mix of hardboiled gangster-movie conventions and hip post-Warhol irony. Starring John Travolta and Samuel L. Jackson. Rated R for violence, language, nudity and drug use.   The Quick and the Dead R   A dead-on western from Sam Raimi (the ``Evil Dead'' movies). Reprising his avuncular villainy from ``Unforgiven,'' Gene Hackman plays the Big Cheese in a tiny western town who, for his own amusement and ego gratification, annually sponsors a quick-draw competition. Among the gathering of gunfighters lured by glory, money or both are a flashy gambler (Lance Henriksen), a pipe-smoking hired gun (Keith David), and Hackman's own son (Leonardo DiCaprio), a likable, cocky kid who really is as good as he says. There's also one of Hackman's ex-cronies (Russell Crowe), who's turned from a man of the gun to a man of God, yet is forced by Hackman to compete (i.e., draw or die). Finally, there's a mysterious stranger (Sharon Stone) with an old score to settle. Stone is getting most of the advance publicity (and yes, she's just fine), but essentially she's just one among a colorful and accomplished cast who knows exactly how to match Raimi's imaginative over-the-top tone. Though the aforementioned ``Unforgiven'' comes to mind, Raimi's true model is Sergio Leone -- especially ``Once Upon a Time in the West.'' One heck of a homage to the spaghetti western, ``The Quick and the Dead'' is flavored with its own homemade sauce.   Red R   The capstone to Krzysztof Kieslowski's exquisite tricolor triptych (after ``Blue'' and ``White''), this simply affecting film centers on the odd relationship that develops between a part-time model (Irene Jacob of ``The Double Life of Veronique'') and a reclusive retired judge (Jean-Louis Trintignant).  </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950210.0076 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-MINI-REVIEWS-(4thtake)-COX) </DATELINE> <TEXT> Speechless PG-13   While it's often true that politics makes strange bedfellows, politically themed romantic comedies often make endearing ones. That's certainly the case here with Geena Davis and Michael Keaton cast as rival speechwriters working for opposing candidates during a senatorial race. Rated PG-13 for language and adult themes.   Strawberries and Chocolate NR   Havana homophobia is the ingredient in a Cuban drama too vanilla for its own good. Think of a Lite ``Kiss of the Spider Woman,'' and you get the picture. Tomas Gutierrez Alea's film follows the edgy nonmating dance between flaming artiste Diego (Jorge Perrugoria) and revolutionary student David (Vladimir Cruz). Diego lures the young man home with the promise of banned books. What he really wants is all too clear in Perrugoria's lip-licking performance. Repelled, David tells his militant roomie that he knew Diego was ``a fag'' by his choice of ice cream: ``There was chocolate, he took strawberry.'' But David visits Diego again with plans to report the man's ``subversive'' activities; and instead finds himself intrigued by Diego's theatrical approach to life. Throw in an unstable hooker next door (Mirta Ibarra) with eyes for David, and you've got the makings of an Almodovar souffle. But ``Strawberry'' is weighted with lard. Set in 1979, it might have had more impact if it had actually been made then. Shot last year, it plays like a passe example of consciousness raising, the lukewarm tale of a noble Nell who makes emotional sacrifices for the stalwart young hetero. Though at least -- unlike William Hurt in ``Spider Woman'' -- Diego doesn't have to die in the process. That's some sort of progress. -- Steve Murray   To Live NR   Famed Asian filmmaker Zhang Yimou (``Raise the Red Lantern'') tackles epic melodrama in this decades-spanning film set in China. Starring Ge You and Gong Li. Not rated, but there are adult themes and some violence.   Vanya on 42nd Street PG   Collaborating with stage director Andre Gregory, Louis Malle has created an ardent love letter -- to the theater, to great acting, to the tragicomic genius of Anton Chekhov. This pared-down performance of Chekhov's ``Uncle Vanya'' celebrates the fleeting glory of live performance as well as the enduring timelessness of the play. Starring Wallace Shawn and Julianne Moore. Rated PG for adult themes. They move weekly to clients of the New York Times News Service.   G: General audiences. Nothing that would offend parents if viewed by children.   PG: Parental guidance suggested. May contain some material parents consider inappropriate for young children.   PG-13: Parents strongly cautioned. Some material may be inappropriate for preteens.   R: Under 17 requires accompanying parent or adult guardian. Parents are urged to learn more about the film before taking children.   NC-17: No one under 17 admitted. Replaces the X rating.   NR: Not rated.   Bad Company R   This designer noir would like to take its place alongside such dark gems as ``Prizzi's Honor,'' ``The Grifters'' and ``The Last Seduction.'' But it's a poseur. As in his Goldie Hawn vehicle ``Deceived'' (1991), director Damian Harris wamps the movie in portentous nocturnal shadows and slows things to a crawl just when the flick needs a good jolt. Laurence Fishburne and Ellen Barkin star. Rated R for strong sexuality, violence and language. -- Steve Murray   Before Sunrise R   Julie Delpy and Ethan Hawke play strangers on a train who become lovers in Vienna, Austria, when she accepts his impetuous invitation to join him for an all-night waltz around the city. It sounds terribly romantic and, at times, it is, but their relationship -- with the audience and each other -- never grows. Director Richard Linklater, whose ``Slacker'' and ``Dazed and Confused'' were such brilliant distillations of a certain generation, has given us little more than a one-night stand to remember.   Billy Madison PG-13   Continuing pop culture's dalliance with idiot chic, this low-watt comedy is the first full- blown star vehicle for ``Saturday Night Live's'' resident smirky boy, Adam Sandler. He plays the title character, a dim-bulb slacker of the manor born who must repeat all 12 grades of school in order to win the respect of his father (Darren McGavin) and gain control of the family's zillion-dollar hotel business (doting dad bought off his teachers the first time around). Sandler is a likable goof with palpable charisma, but ``Billy Madison's'' feature-film length exposes the chinks in his act. Blessed with a none-too-vast repertoire of scrunchy faces and kooky voices, the comedian simply runs out of ideas after a while. It's hard to imagine him as an enduring screen presence. Then again, stranger things have happened (see: Pauly Shore). Rated PG-13 for language and sexual innuendo. -- Doug Hamilton   Blue NR   Krzysztof Kieslowski's coolly stunning mood piece stars Juliette Binoche (``Damage'') as a young widow. After her husband -- a world-famous composer -- and daughter are killed in a car crash, Binoche cuts herself off from family, friends, lovers and, most crucially, any semblance of the passionate, creative person she was before the tragedy (it's hinted she wrote her husband's music). Binoche brings a lovely stillness to her role, leaving the emotionalism to director Kieslowski, whose lush soundtrack and immaculate images have a hypnotic pull.   Blue Sky PG-13   Jessica Lange snagged an Oscar nomination for her performance in this riveting family drama set against a backdrop of military cover-ups and abuse of power. Directed by Tony Richardson. Rated PG-13 for sex, brief nudity and adult themes.   Boys on the Side R   Whoopi Goldberg, Drew Barrymore and Mary-Louise Parker are smack in the middle of a highly entertaining New Age weepie. Though the on-the-road ambience suggests ``Thelma &AMP; Louise'' (Whoopi even makes a crack about ``not driving off a cliff with you two!''), this movie's true model is ``Terms of Endearment.'' Director Herbert Ross isn't exactly the cutting-edge type, but he knows how to handle actors and he's not afraid to take the material on its own terms -- as a hip tear-jerker, as agile as it is blubbery. This isn't a profound film or even a risky one, but it's solid through and through. Rated R for language and adult themes.   The Brady Bunch Movie PG-13   Stick with the reruns. To their credit, the cast of l ook- alikes -- headed by Shelley Long and Gary Cole as Carol and Mike, Christine Taylor as Marcia, Christopher Daniel Barnes as Greg and Jennifer Elise Cox as Jan -- play it straight and true. But to what purpose? What does it take to parody something that was pretty pathetic in its original form? As in the previous big-screen versions of ``The Beverly Hillbillies,'' ``The Addams Family'' and a half-dozen other recycled sitcoms, the plot calls for fictional TV characters to collide with the ``real'' world. In this case, an evil neighbor (Michael McKeon) wants to bulldoze the Brady's home, but they love every shag-carpeted inch of it and refuse to sell. The '70s vs. the '90s jokes are repeated ad nauseum, but there are a few good yuks at Marcia's expense (especially regarding the insanely jealous Jan). ``Brady'' is dull, safe, just more of the same-old, same-old; John Waters had much more fun covering the same territory in ``Serial Mom.'' If the filmmakers had really wanted to shake things up, they should've had loyal housekeeper Alice go after little Bobby. Or little Cindy.  </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950217.0061 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-MINI-REVIEWS-(2ndtake)-COX) </DATELINE> <TEXT> Clear and Present Danger PG-13   Even when this new installment in the Tom Clancy film-adaptation franchise is far from clear, it's dangerously entertaining. Reteaming director Phillip Noyce and star Harrison Ford as CIA analyst Jack Ryan, it's more convoluted than ``Patriot Games.'' But also more gripping. Rated PG-13 for some intense action, violence and language. -- Steve Murray   Disclosure R   Michael Crichton's provocative best seller about a female boss (Demi Moore) who manhandles -- so to speak -- a male underling (Michael Douglas) and then charges him with sexual harassment isn't a bad setup for a movie. But, thanks to director Barry Levinson's ham-fisted approach, the material has been reduced to the level of ``Basic Instinct''/``Fatal Attraction'' trash. Rated R for sex and language.   Dumb and Dumber PG-13   The final entry in Jim Carrey's Doofus Trilogy '94, after mega-hits ``Ace Ventura: Pet Detective'' and ``The Mask.'' Easily the most lowbrow ridiculous of the three, ``Dumb'' is barely a movie at all -- it's more like a visual volume of ``Truly Tasteless Jokes'' occasionally interrupted by the most anemic suspense plot imaginable. It's also a riot. Rated PG-13 for language and adult situations. -- Doug Hamilton   Erotique NR   One person's sex fantasy is another's so-what. This anthology of sex tales, directed by a trio of female directors, isn't overtly offensive. However, it is overtly boring. You'd have to a a pretty sex- starved individual -- say, you hadn't gotten any since Eisenhower -- to find these pieces a turn-on. The first, directed by the erratic Lizzie Borden (``Working Girls'' good; ``Love Crimes'' bad), is about a struggling actress (Kamala Lopez-Dawson) who pays the rent working as a phone-sex operator. In the second, directed by Monica Trent, two glamorous lesbians (Priscilla Barnes and Camilla Soeberg) decide to pick up a Eurotrash stud (Michael Carr) for their delectation. Finally, there's Clara Law's rather sweet Hong Kong-set sex comedy about an assimilated Asian guy (Tim Lounibos) who wants to win his girl's (Hayley Man) heart by appealing to her heritage. The idea of an all-girl team of directors is a condescending tease. By relying on PC bully tactics -- these are female fantasies -- the film gets away with being little more than run-of- the-mill soft-core porn (phony moans and bad acting). And while the recurring theme of women on top isn't bad in itself, the movie sends an ugly message: Dominant women inevitably need men who are helpless, humiliated, or dead. And that is certainly not what most women want.   The Adventures   of Yellow Dog PG   Set in the spectacular forests of British Columbia, this pleasant family picture is a straightforward tale of a boy and his dog. Only this boy (Jesse Bradford from ``King of the Hill'') and this dog -- a soulful blond lab named Dakotah -- are lost together in the Canadian wilderness. Rated PG for mild violence.   Forrest Gump PG-13   Tom Hanks is clueless in America in Robert Zemeckis' half-baked fable about an idiot-savant of sorts, skimming through the last half of the 20th century and colliding ``Zelig''-like with history. Rated PG-13 for adult themes.   Heavyweights PG   While it may not be a ton o' fun, pound for pound, this Disney flick about a camp for hefty- size kids is a perfectly pleasant time-passer. Initially, the premise sounds a little crummy, but as one veteran (Atlanta native, Keenan Thompson) explains to rookie Aaron Schwartz (a ``Mighty Ducks'' alum), ``No one picks on you, because you're not the fat kid; everybody's the fat kid.'' Unfortunately, the camp has changed hands and the new owner (Ben Stiller) is a pumped-up psychopath who intends to use his corpulent campers as the unlikely -- and unwilling -- stars of his new weight-loss video. Turn the camera on and he's an energized evangelist for veggies, jogging and clean living; turn it off and he's the personal trainer from hell. The movie eventually succumbs to the usual Disney formula of ``go for it'' overkill (there are at least two climaxes too many), but Stiller is very buff, very funny and the kids make fine foils. Holding it all together is Tom McGowan who wowed Broadway a few years back in the faux- Moliere, ``La Bete.'' Here, he's thoroughly engaging as an overweight counselor who learns he can stand up against the Stillers of the world.   Higher Learning R   John Singleton's third film proves that trouble is just as prevalent outside the hood in ``Higher Learning.'' Set on a fictional, racially mixed university campus, Malik Williams (Omar Epps), Kristen Connor (Kristy Swanson) and Remy (Michael Rapaport) face race and social relations at their worst, all in the course of one year. When the final bell rings, ``Higher Learning'' is as emotionally taxing as tonight's blood-drenched evening newscast. Both are stirring, true. But one's free. Rated R for offensive language, nudity and violence. -- Sonia Murray   Immortal Beloved R   As visually lush as it is musically glorious, this clever conjecture on Beethoven's love life emphasizes entertainment, not accuracy. Gary Oldman stars. -- Steve Murray   In the Mouth   of Madness R   Sam Neill plays a private investigator hired to find a missing best-selling horror writer named Sutter Cane (Jurgen Prochnow) in this rip-roaring flick from John Carpenter, who practically reinvented the horror film with ``Halloween.'' Carpenter still knows his way around scary stuff -- from dead-eyed children to ax-wielding maniacs. And he even leaves us with a message worth pondering: When the world goes mad, the mad go to the movies. Rated R for violence, language and gore.   Interview With the Vampire R   As richly textured as a dark brocade, threaded with existential dread and drenched in plasma, Neil Jordan's film will disturb many viewers. It's filled with snapping necks, slit throats, rats and wrists draining crimson into goblets, and other bloody bits of necro- fun. But it's likely to thrill Anne Rice fans and seduce those unfamiliar with her books. Starring Brad Pitt and Tom Cruise. Rated R for violence, gore, nudity and sexuality. -- Steve Murray  </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950217.0062 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-MINI-REVIEWS-(3rdtake)-COX) </DATELINE> <TEXT> I.Q. PG-13   Directed by Fred Schepisi (``Roxanne''), this comedy about the romance between hearty mechanic Ed (Tim Robbins) and heady mathematician Catherine (Meg Ryan) zips by with sparkle. It's not a 4.0, but ``I.Q.'' scores high enough to pass. Rated PG for mild language and adult themes. -- Steve Murray   The Jerky Boys R   With their phone-crank outrages, the Jerky Boys personify the urban id unleashed. Ranting about hemorrhoids and all things anal, they shatter the daily grind with scatological rudeness. Too bad they're being packaged in this pathetic showcase, a tired, Disneyfied chase comedy involving the mob and crooked cops. Rated R for strong language. -- Steve Murray   The Jungle Book PG   For kids, there's a bunch of cool animals. For adults, there's Jason Scott Lee in a loincloth. But for Rudyard Kipling fans, there's not much. It's like director Stephen Sommers' ``Adventures of Huck Finn'' last year -- another example of great source material that lacked an animating spark. -- Steve Murray   Just Cause R   By the end you can count the cliches: Yes, the brutal rapist- killer will kidnap the blonde, evade his pursuers via the standard drive-in-the- wrong-lane-of-traffic car chase, and then flee to exactly the spot police expect him to go. In this formula thriller, Sean Connery plays a Harvard law professor who reopens the case of a Florida death-row inmate (Blair Underwood) set to fry for the rape and murder of a young (white) girl. Laurence Fishburne is the local detective who tries to intimidate Connery out of town. As packed with crackers as a bowl of oyster stew, the film's small-town setting reeks of the socially conscious flicks of the '60s (``In the Heat of the Night''). Forget whodunit; you may be distracted by the movie's racial-sexual politics. With its hackneyed view of the South, it cues us to see Underwood's imprisonment as a symbolic punishment for a black male's taboo violation of a white female (whether he did it or not). Yet by the convoluted end, the movie all but endorses the same distasteful stereotype it pretends to explode. Still, Connery and Fishburne are predictably fine, and Ed Harris as a corn-pone Hannibal Lecter clone jumps on his Bible-spouting part with deep-fried relish. -- Steve Murray   The Last Seduction R   Probably the sleeper of the year. In the tradition of ``Double Indemnity'' and ``Body Heat,'' director John Dahl (``Red Rock West'') pairs a smart, sexy, no-good dame with a hunky sucker who's not half as smart as he thinks he is. Linda Fiorentino is a one-woman wrecking crew as a tough cookie with a lotta leg and even more nerve who easily gets nice-guy Peter Berg in her thrall. Rated R for sex, violence, language, nudity and adult themes.   Legends of the Fall R   Think of the very best ``Bonanza'' episode you ever saw; then cast it with the likes of Anthony Hopkins, Brad Pitt and Aidan Quinn; finally add in the sibling rivalry/father-always-liked-you-best themes of ``East of Eden.'' Yummy. Rated R for sex, nudity and violence.   The Lion King G   Disney's feature-length cartoon is royal entertainment. Sort of ``Bambi'' meets ``Hamlet'' on the Serengeti, it roars onto the screen with vivid characters and expert animation. Featuring the voices of James Earl Jones, Jeremy Irons and Matthew Broderick. Rated G, but there is a death that may disturb young children.   Little Giants PG   This time, the Bad News Bears . . . I mean, the Mighty Ducks . . . I mean . . . Let's start over. This time, a gang of lovable kiddie misfits pull together and become a heck of a Pee Wee League football team. The only discernable twist is that the Giants' coach (Rick Moranis) and the coach of the all-star opposing team (Ed O'Neill) are brothers with a longstanding rivalry. Given a fourth-down-and-long situation, director Duwayne Dunham punts for formula and lets his cast fumble along as best they can. Rated PG for mild language.   Little Women PG   Director Gillian Armstrong (``My Brilliant Career'') and her solid cast, including Winona Ryder and Susan Sarandon, have imbued the third screen version of Louisa May Alcott's classic with considerable care and craftsmanship -- every scene looks like a Currier and Ives print. Rated PG for mild adult themes.   A Low Down Dirty Shame R   Racist, sexist, homophobic and rude, Keenen Ivory Wayan's new action comedy is also shamelessly entertaining, turning genre cliches into a romp. Rated R for violence, strong language and sex. -- Steve Murray   The Madness   of King George NR   The year is 1788. King George III (Nigel Hawthorne) already has lost the Colonies. Now he's losing his mind. Based on Alan Bennett's much-praised play and artfully transferred from the stage by Nicholas Hytner, this historical whimsy recalls ``The Lion in Winter'' or ``The Ruling Class.'' But Hawthorne's extraordinary portrayal gives ``Madness'' its own unforgettable voice. He's definitely Oscar worthy.   The Mask PG-13   Forget the superhero plot of this live-action cartoon and focus on the Looney-Tunes special effects, the immensely likable Jim Carrey and Milo the mighty Jack Russell terrier. Directed by Chuck Russell. Rated PG-13 for mild violence.   Miami Rhapsody PG-13   Percolating to a mix of heady one-liners and romantic angst, writer- director David Frankel's disarmingly witty comedy examines the pitfalls of love (and lust) in the '90s. Sarah Jessica Parker is the film's invaluable motor, equally adept at spewing Mensa-level quips and dipping into slapstick. Frankel peppers his script with so many zingers, you want to curb your laughter so as not to miss the next one. Rated PG-13 for language and adult themes. -- Steve Murray  </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950217.0063 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-MINI-REVIEWS-(4thtake)-COX) </DATELINE> <TEXT> Murder in the First R   The more or less true story of a history-making case. In 1941, after spending years in solitary confinement for attempted escape (the usual maximum was 19 days), Alcatraz inmate Henri Young (Kevin Bacon) killed the convict who'd ratted on him in front of 200 witnesses. An ambitious young attorney (Christian Slater) took the case and decided to put the system on trial. This is good material and the actors give it their best. Alas, director Marc Rocco is his own -- and their -- worst enemy. His distinctively active camera has a certain panache, but it soon becomes an irritating affectation that detracts from the performances and the story. Rated R for violence, nudity, language, sex.   Nell PG-13   A gentle miracle of a movie with an Oscar-worthy performance by Jodie Foster. She plays the title character: a feral backwoods North Carolina woman who's been raised in almost total isolation from the modern world (No phone, no TV, etc.). Liam Neeson and Natasha Richardson are rival doctors who become fascinated by her. Rated PG-13 for nudity.   Nobody's Fool R   Even when director Robert Benton grabs for the heartstrings, star Paul Newman tempers the sentiment and gives a career-capping performance as a father at the crossroads. Rated R for language and nudity. -- Steve Murray   Pulp Fiction R   Pop goes the pulp in this smashing sophomore effort by Quentin ``Reservoir Dogs'' Tarantino. Winner of the Golden Palm at Cannes and already subject to Oscar-time whispers, the picture is a gleefully lurid mix of hardboiled gangster-movie conventions and hip post-Warhol irony. Starring John Travolta and Samuel L. Jackson. Rated R for violence, language, nudity and drug use.   The Quick and the Dead R   A dead-on western from Sam Raimi (the ``Evil Dead'' movies). Reprising his avuncular villainy from ``Unforgiven,'' Gene Hackman plays the Big Cheese in a tiny western town who, for his own amusement and ego gratification, annually sponsors a quick-draw competition. Among the gathering of gunfighters lured by glory, money or both are a flashy gambler (Lance Henriksen), a pipe- smoking hired gun (Keith David), and Hackman's own son (Leonardo DiCaprio), a likable, cocky kid who really is as good as he says. There's also one of Hackman's ex-cronies (Russell Crowe), who's turned from a man of the gun to a man of God, yet is forced by Hackman to compete (i.e., draw or die.) Finally, there's a mysterious stranger (Sharon Stone) with an old score to settle. Stone is getting most of the advance publicity (and yes, she's just fine), but essentially she's just one among a colorful and accomplished cast who knows exactly how to match Raimi's imaginative over-the-top tone. Though the aforementioned ``Unforgiven'' comes to mind, Raimi's true model is Sergio Leone -- especially ``Once Upon a Time in the West.'' One heck of a homage to the spaghetti western, ``The Quick and the Dead'' is flavored with its own homemade sauce. Rated R for violence and language.   Quiz Show PG-13   Robert Redford directs a dazzingly entertaining movie about the quiz-show scandals of the '50s. The medium's message: Forget it, Jake; it's television. Starring Ralph Fiennes, John Turturro and Rob Morrow. Rated PG-13 for adult themes.   Richie Rich PG   Another chance for Macaulay Culkin to smirk. As comic-to-celluloid mutations go, it's what you'd expect: thin on script, fat on sets (the Riches' abode is Asheville's Biltmore Estate). The film offers a spectacularly twisted message from filmmakers wanting to play both sides of the fiscal fence. After endorsing the Riches' dollar-happy delirium, the movie claims that Richie's true wealth is a new set of friends (a Rainbow Coalition of street urchins enticed to come play with his expensive toys). Rated PG for mild violence and language. -- Steve Murray   Stargate PG-13   An engaging sci-fi yarn with a flying pyramid, three moons and Jaye Davidson in a dress. Again. We start on Earth, where a linguist named Daniel (James Spader) deciphers Egyptian hieroglyphics on a circular mechanism unearthed near the Pyramids. It's the Stargate, a thingamajig that, when uncoded, becomes a portal to a twin gate on a sandy planet across the universe. Daniel gets enlisted for a space-jumping expedition, led by gruff Colonel Jack O'Neil (Kurt Russell), since he's the only one who can crack the code on that second Stargate. But first they have to face the sun god Ra (Davidson), a cosmic vampire. Like a Tintin adventure set in the cosmos, the movie has an enjoyable retro feel. But it's harder to get revved up by it. Rated PG-13 for violence. -- Steve Murray  </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950217.0064 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-MINI-REVIEWS-(5thtake)-COX) </DATELINE> <TEXT> Strawberry and Chocolate R   Havana homophobia is the ingred ient in a Cuban drama too vanilla for its own good. Think of a Lite ``Kiss of the Spider Woman,'' and you get the picture. Tomas Gutierrez Alea's film follows the edgy nonmating dance between flaming artiste Diego (Jorge Perrugoria) and revolutionary student David (Vladimir Cruz). Diego lures the young man home with the promise of banned books. What he really wants is all too clear in Perrugoria's lip-licking performance. Repelled, David tells his militant roomie that he knew Diego was ``a fag'' by his choice of ice cream: ``There was chocolate, he took strawberry.'' But David visits Diego again with plans to report the man's ``subversive'' activities; and instead finds himself intrigued by Diego's theatrical approach to life. Throw in an unstable hooker next door (Mirta Ibarra) with eyes for David, and you've got the makings of an Almodovar souffle. But ``Strawberry'' is weighted with lard. Set in 1979, it might have had more impact if it had actually been made then. Shot last year, it plays like a passe example of consciousness raising, the lukewarm tale of a noble Nell who makes emotional sacrifices for the stalwart young hetero. Though at least -- unlike William Hurt in ``Spider Woman'' -- Diego doesn't have to die in the process. That's some sort of progress. Rated R for language and strong sexuality. -- Steve Murray   Tom and Viv PG-13   With its genteel English settings, its repressed famous writer and his couldn't-be-more-different spouse, this story of T.S. Eliot and his first wife, Vivian Haigh-Wood, comes off as something of a ``Shadowlands'' wannabe. However, director Brian Gilbert and writer Michael Hastings haven't quite made a love story; worse, they're not quite sure what they have made. Viv (Miranda Richardson) is a vivacious heiress; Tom (Willem Dafoe) is a poor Yank at Oxford who falls for her country manse, her family, her sheer Englishness. But as Viv's brother (Tim Dutton) notes, ``She thought Tom was going to rescue her from tight little England, but tight little England was just what Tom wanted.'' Their marriage is a sad mismatch -- as her high spirits spill overboard into high-strung neuroticism and neediness, he becomes more withdrawn. But the film does little more than skim through their dilemma. If you didn't already know ``The Waste Land'' or ``Prufrock,'' you'd think Tom was an obnoxious prig, not worth Viv's trouble. Conversely, though her mother (Rosemary Harris in a strong supporting role) hints of ``female trouble,'' we never get more than Viv as a generic society loony. The film has moments of genuine emotional connection, but overall, this is a hollow movie.   Vanya on 42nd Street PG   Collaborating with stage director Andre Gregory, Louis Malle has created an ardent love letter -- to the theater, to great acting, to the tragicomic genius of Anton Chekhov. This pared- down performance of Chekhov's ``Uncle Vanya'' celebrates the fleeting glory of live performance as well as the enduring timelessness of the play. Starring Wallace Shawn and Julianne Moore. Rated PG for adult themes. .  </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950217.0065 type=story > <HEADLINE> 50 YEARS LATER, IWO JIMA VETERANS REMEMBER </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> ATLANTA  (BC-IWOJIMA-COX) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_199502.gz"
