(--Europe and Asia: KARL HORWITZ in Paris at 47-42-17-11; fax, 47-42-80-44 or 47-42-18-81; telex, 282-942. (--Mexico, Central America, South America: PAUL FINCH in Los Angeles, 310-996-0075; fax, 310-996-0089. Includes a 100-word sidebar. By WILLIAM STEPHEN CROSS c.1996 William Stephen Cross (Distributed by New York Times Syndicate.)  It's 12:30 a.m. on a Friday, an hour when most normal adults are winding down with Letterman or finishing off a nightcap before heading home. I'm asking myself what a 45-year-old family man with a postgraduate degree is doing on the ice with 30 other men and women dressed up in plastic armor and carrying wooden, laminate and aluminum sticks?  Why, waiting for the referee to drop the puck. It hits the ice and it's skate 'til you drop _ two hours of fast-paced rushes, stick-handling and the occasional goal.  Not the fastest one on the ice, I try parking myself beside our opponent's net. Sure enough, a loose puck slides along the ice to my feet, and without pausing to think about it, I shovel it in. Suddenly I no longer feel the late hour or my sore muscles, I've scored my first goal!  I'm not alone in my jubilation. Adult ice hockey has quietly become the fastest growing sport in North America and Europe, especially in the over-35 age group. NUMBERS ON ICE  The loose, informal organization of most teams and leagues means no one has precise figures, but various estimates put the number of adults in hockey leagues at 250,000 and growing by 15 percent each year. These figures are probably far higher if pick-up games, club hockey and other casual contests could be tracked.  Fans of the professional game, who once thought it was too late for them to play because they never played as kids, have discovered that nothing is more electrifying than watching that little piece of vulcanized rubber stretch the twine at the back of the net.  Operators of skating arenas report that the recent boom in construction of ice rinks simply can't keep pace with the demand at the adult and child level. Teams often happen when a group of friends get together and rent some ice time, or get assembled at arenas into ``house leagues'' with seasons and play-offs.  There's even an international league, Hockey North America (HNA) with over 10,000 members of both genders in over 25 cities. HNA also has a sister league in Holland called Ice Hockey Europe, and the sport is flourishing in Germany, Denmark, Sweden, Finland, Switzerland and even northern Italy.  Adult hockey players come from all walks of life. A stockbroker, who identifies himself as ``Andrew'' (lest his boss discover he's spending a long lunch hour playing hockey), admitted to keeping a beeper wrapped in a towel on the bench.  ``I have sell orders on file at the office from all my clients,'' he says, ``so I can protect them in case the market goes into a tailspin while I'm on the ice.''  While it's true that far more men than women play, many teams have at least one female player. And while women are not normally considered physically aggressive, this reporter was dumped quite rudely in front of the net during a game by a female forward. As the referee skated up ice with the play, she deftly pulled my skates out from under me. GETTING STARTED  Anyone interested in playing for the first time will progress faster if they can already skate, but it's certainly not a requirement.  Hockey North America, for example, has a beginner course designed for people who have never gotten any closer to a game than their TV set. It starts with the fundamentals, then moves on to a 10-game season complete with playoffs.  After that, better players and teams can choose from three levels of play. House leagues often accept all comers, rarely making any effort to assign players based on ability.  Season costs average around $400, but vary by location, usually according to the price for ice-rink rental. Regions with fewer rinks tend to be higher than those with more venues.  Playing hockey calls for short bursts of intense activity, followed by periods spent on the bench. Those starting out should get into shape first. In-line skating or bicycling (both the stationary and the outdoor variety) help improve conditioning in the critical leg muscles and heart. It's also a good idea to have a complete check-up with your family physician before entering into any strenuous exercise regimen. REALITY CHECK  While professional hockey has a decided image problem because of fighting, most adult leagues discourage or even ban fisticuffs outright, as well as body checking, the process of separating the puck from the player by ramming him into the side boards.  This doesn't mean there isn't contact, especially among beginner players still mastering the art of stopping. But players who lose their tempers get tossed out of games, and persistent offenders usually get banned.  Perhaps in emulation of their National Hockey League heroes, some players occasionally end up in shoving matches or exchanges of verbal unpleasantries. When the puck's up for grabs around the goal, pileups are common, causing outbursts of temper and frustration. But most players will agree that having to explain a black eye from a hockey fight to a spouse or boss is a rarity.  Like many other players, my own shiny new hockey career began after watching how much fun my 7-year-old son had on the ice. I signed up for an adult instruction course at a local rink, then played pickup games until I felt I was ready. After that, a phone call to a league and an exchange of money got me assigned to the Gamblers.  My initial excitement at ``making the team'' was tempered a bit, though, when I found out they'd gone 0-32 over the past three seasons. But in the dressing room at our first game, all the players were friendly and enthusiastic _ a mood that slipped noticeably after the team absorbed an 11-1 thrashing.  The Gamblers' record of flawless imperfection continued through the next five games. But my goal was never winning a championship, it was to play the game I've always loved watching.  Whatever happens now, I've won a victory of sorts, a victory over aging, my inhibitions and self-doubts. Can Wayne Gretzky claim that? SIDEBAR: THE PUCK STOPS HERE  Getting started:  Almost every rink now has adult ice hockey programs; check the yellow pages for the nearest ice skating rink. The largest single league is Hockey North America. For information, call 800-4-HOCKEY.  Cost: A 20-game season (90 minutes per game) with double elimination playoffs costs around $400, depending on your location (ice time costs vary by region).  What you need: Most programs require (in addition to hockey skates) a regulation helmet, gloves, elbow and shoulder pads, pants and shin guards. Men will want to include an athletic supporter with a cup.  Depending on the brand and quality of the equipment, expect to spend at least $400 to $500 for a complete set; this figure does not include skates ($100-$350) or sticks ($18 for an inexpensive wooden one up to $100 for a graphite model with replaceable blade).  Some stores now feature used equipment, and many rinks have bulletin boards offering equipment for sale. Goalie equipment is much more expensive (pads can run as much as $1,500 new), and is best purchased used.  Visors or full face shields are highly recommended. Don't skimp on your equipment _ even with no checking allowed, collisions and spills demand solid protection. (--Europe and Asia: KARL HORWITZ in Paris at 47-42-17-11; fax, 47-42-80-44 or 47-42-18-81; telex, 282-942. (--Mexico, Central America, South America: PAUL FINCH in Los Angeles, 310-996-0075; fax, 310-996-0089. By BILL LAWREN c.1996 Longevity (Distributed by New York Times Syndicate.)  Once-a-day supplements such as vitamins C and E and beta-carotene aren't the only things flying off the shelves these days. Health-minded shoppers are filling their baskets with more esoteric _ but no less potent _ over-the-counter mind, body and even sex-life rechargers such as melatonin and glutathione.  What's more, many are finding doctors who will write prescriptions for still more powerful _ and controversial _ elixirs.  How come? Quite simply, if you don't replenish these hormones, proteins, antioxidants and other vital substances that start to dwindle as early as age 30, you lose them.  The good news: Your body's crucial chemicals can be restored _ often with simple and safe tactics such as exercise, diet and dietary supplements. Hard-core longevity seekers can ask their doctors about hormone replacement or prescription drugs.  What follows is a guide to today's hot rejuvenation supplements.  A word of caution: Don't start any restorative therapy program without first discussing it with your doctor. Acetylcholine: The Memory Pill  As acetylcholine (ACh) runs low, mental function may take a hit, especially memory. Choline or lecithin supplements can help boost ACh levels.  In a recent study at Florida International University, people over 50 who took 13.5 grams of lecithin a day for five weeks went from 35 memory lapses a week to 19. Cardiolipin: The Brain Booster  Cardiolipin protects mitochondria _ the tiny ``energy plants'' for every cell in the body. Many experts think mitochondrial slowdown underlies every aspect of aging _ so cardiolipin may be a key to aging itself.  A cardiolipin replenisher may be on the way: Acetyl-L-carnitine (ALCAR), now being tested by Sigma Tau, Inc., for treating Alzheimer's disease, boosts cardiolipin in old rats.  ALCAR is available in Europe, where it's widely prescribed not only for Alzheimer's but to slow normal brain aging. DHEA: All-Purpose Youth Tonic  Dubbed the ``mother of all hormones'' (its offspring include the sex hormones estrogen and androgens), DHEA may also buffer the effects of stress hormones and prevent age-related diseases.  ``DHEA levels are a fairly significant predictor of longevity,'' says Stanford University biologist Robert Sapolsky  Low DHEA has been linked to a number of diseases, including breast cancer, obesity, high cholesterol, hypertension and heart disease. Even under normal circumstances, DHEA levels decline by as much as 75 percent between early adulthood and old age.  In animals, DHEA replacement works wonders, fighting obesity, protecting against diabetes, sharpening memory and warding off infectious diseases. But in humans DHEA supplementation is controversial. Though it's long been used in Europe as an anti-obesity and anti-aging drug, some scientists warn high doses may lead to liver damage and excessive testosterone levels.  On the plus side, when Dr. Samuel S.C. Yen and his colleagues at the University of California School of Medicine in La Jolla gave 50 milligrams of DHEA daily for six months to 13 men and 17 women ages 40 to 70, they reported ``a remarkable increase'' in physical and psychological well-being, deeper sleep and an improved ability to handle stress.  DHEA is very potent and should not be taken without consulting your doctor. Dopamine: Smart Medicine  It's long been known that Parkinson's disease patients have extra-low levels of the neurotransmitter dopamine. Now studies suggest low dopamine may be part of normal aging, especially in men, and may play a role in memory loss.  Prescription dopamine boosters such as levo-dopa, bromocriptine and selegeline (Deprenyl) are used to treat Parkinson's. Can they also help the healthy?  A 1992 study by Dr. Richard A. Depue and his colleagues at the University of Minnesota Medical School showed that eight 20- to 35-year-old women who took 2.5 milligram doses of bromocriptine scored an average of 44 percent higher on spatial memory tests than women who took only a placebo. Estrogen: The Sexy Mood Lifter  The ``female hormone'' helps regulate reproduction functions in both sexes and is essential for growth and bone development. It may also be involved in mood (low levels are linked to PMS and postnatal depression), memory and even strength, balance and coordination.  The debate over estrogen replacement therapy (ERT) is fierce. On the plus side, estrogen can lower cardiac risk by boosting ``good'' HDL cholesterol and lowering ``bad'' LDL. Estrogen replacement also helps maintain bone mass and lowers fracture risk in women with osteoporosis. It fights menopause symptoms such as ``hot flashes.''  The downside is ERT may raise breast cancer risk. But studies have been conflicting on this. Glutathione: Nature's Health Insurance  Not as hyped as vitamins C and E and beta-carotene, glutathione is a heavyweight antioxidant. It's also a major natural detoxifier, neutralizing environmental poisons and carcinogens.  Some experts hold that glutathione levels decline as we age. People with age-related diseases such as heart disease, arthritis and Type II diabetes have lower levels of glutathione than healthy people.  According to one study, ``elderly people with high blood glutathione levels are physically, psychologically and sociologically healthier.''  Glutathione is sold in health-food stores, but you can also get it in foods such as fresh, unprocessed pork; veal; chicken and even ground beef. Fresh whole fruit and vegetables (not juices) are also high in glutathione, especially citrus fruits, melons and raw carrots. GH: The Hardbody Hormone  The potent human growth hormone (GH) is one of the most controversial anti-agers. In children and adolescents, the hormone is essential for growth and physical development. In adults it affects how lean or flabby we are, and may boost the immune system.  GH declines start in young adulthood and accelerate with age. Some adults _ especially ones with more sedentary lifestyles _ may produce no GH at all. GH enthusiasts link its decline to the shrinking muscles and loss of strength that often accompany aging.  In early studies, GH seemed to be an awesome anti-aging supplement _ older people who took it lost fat, gained muscle and had improved skin tone.  But recent trials raise doubts: The benefits of GH seem modest and brief, with such side effects as water retention and insulin resistance.  On the other hand, a 1991 study at the University of Innsbruck Medical School in Austria reported that GH had simulated immune system cells that fight off bacterial infections.  You can boost GH levels naturally with vigorous exercise such as weight training. As a drug, GH is approved by the FDA only for the treatment of unusually short children. Take this drug only after consulting a doctor. Melatonin: Miracle Body Mender  Rapidly emerging as a major anti-ager, melatonin is a powerful antioxidant and a regulator of the body's clock, causing sleepiness in response to darkness. It also primes the immune system to fight infectious diseases and perhaps even cancer.  Some scientists say that as we age, free radicals cripple melatonin-making cells in the pineal gland. People over 65 have only one-fourth the melatonin levels of 25 year olds. The results: declining immune function, sleep disturbances and possibly increased vulnerability to cancer.  Melatonin supplements are sold in health-food stores. Myofibrillar Proteins: Prime Muscle Builders  Without this family of proteins, we'd be immobile. They underlie the muscle connections that drive all movement.  Dr. Stephen Welle of the University of Rochester found that production of myofibrillar protein in men over 62 was up to 44 percent slower than in men ages 21 to 31. This may explain the loss of muscle size and strength associated with aging.  The key to boosting muscle proteins is exercise, specifically weight training. Testosterone: The Sex-Drive Stimulant  The ``male hormone'' (a misnomer: Women produce it too) is crucial to growth, sexual maturation and perhaps maintenance of sex drive in later years.  Experts think that testosterone declines in one-third of men over 55 _ perhaps contributing to the loss of strength, sagging sexual desire and depression.  In the last five years studies have shown that in men over 54, testosterone replacement can boost lean body mass, strength and sex drive.  Although once testosterone was believed to hasten heart disease, more research shows it actually increases HDL, reducing heart risk. Thymosins: Immunity Boosters  Helping regulate and stimulate the immune system, these hormones also spur the production of neurotransmitters and sex hormones.  The thymus gland, which makes thymosins, shrinks from walnut- size in youth to pea-size in mature adulthood. By old age the gland has virtually disappeared, producing no thymosins.  Thymosins are routinely prescribed in Europe to treat cancer and diabetes. In the United States, alpha-thymosin is FDA-approved for the treatment of hepatitis B.  But you may not have to take thymosins to restore their activity: At least one study has shown that supplements of 400 milligrams of zinc sulfate per day can produce the same results. (--Europe and Asia: KARL HORWITZ in Paris at 47-42-17-11; fax, 47-42-80-44 or 47-42-18-81; telex, 282-942. (--Mexico, Central America, South America: PAUL FINCH in Los Angeles, 310-996-0075; fax, 310-996-0089. Includes a 200-word sidebar By STACEY COLINO From Child magazine c.1996 Stacey Colino (Distributed by New York Times Syndicate.)  Each year in the United States an estimated 16 million children require treatment at emergency rooms, and of the 600,000 that are hospitalized, an astounding 23,000 die because of injuries.  But the majority of these injuries could be prevented, according to pediatric health-care providers.  Here are some lifesaving lessons from ER doctors and emergency medical service (EMS) workers to help you prepare for unexpected emergencies.  Lesson 1: Don't put off taking CPR.  Learning to perform pediatric cardiopulmonary resuscitation can save your child's life if he stops breathing. Courses are offered (usually with basic first aid techniques) at local hospitals and Red Cross centers, and through the American Heart Association.  Lesson 2: Always put your child in a safety seat or a seat belt.  Car crashes are the No. 1 cause of death and disability among children.  Car seats and seat belts could have prevented many of those injuries. Yet often parents who strap themselves in make the mistake of holding a child in their laps, believing that if there is an accident, they'll be able to hold onto the child.  ``That's impossible even at low speeds of 20 or 30 miles per hour,'' says Dr. John P. Santamaria, attending physician at the Pediatric Emergency Center at St. Joseph's Hospital in Tampa, Fla.  For small infants or low-birthweight babies, the American Academy of Pediatrics (AAP) in Chicago recommends strapping infants in an infant car seat that faces the rear of the car. A convertible seat that faces forward should be used for children who weigh more than 20 pounds or are a year old.  A booster seat that properly positions the seat belt is best for kids who have outgrown convertible seats. Once a child is tall enough to wear a shoulder strap and a lap belt comfortably he's safest in the backseat.  Lesson 3: Remove small objects from a young child's environment.  Take a close look at your child's surroundings from her point of view (by getting down on your hands and knees) to remove any hazards.  If your child swallows an object, observe her symptoms before acting. If she's not breathing because the object is obstructing her airway and she's over a year old, use the Heimlich maneuver and call 911, Santamaria advises.  But if she's under 1, coughing and choking slightly but getting air in, don't touch her _ instead, call an ambulance. Patting her on the back, doing the Heimlich maneuver or upsetting her can create a total obstruction of the airway, so it's better to have an emergency medical technician come to your home to treat her.  If your child swallows an object and seems to be none the worse for it, call your pediatrician or local ER for advice. Some objects, such as button batteries, can pose a problem because of their potential to leak, so they need to be removed. Other items may safely make it through your child's digestive tract without intervention.  Lesson 4: Poison-proof your home.  Approximately 32,000 kids under the age of 15 were hospitalized because of poisoning in 1991.  As far as prevention goes, here are the basics:  _ Put child-proof locks on cabinets;  _ use child-proof containers for medications, vitamins and alcohol;  _ keep household chemicals locked up. Always be on the lookout for household hazards, such as peeling paint, that your child could eat. And never put medicines or chemicals in food containers.  If your child ingests a toxin, call your local poison control center before doing anything else. It's smart to keep ipecac syrup in your medicine cabinet, but in some instances, making a child vomit may do more harm than good, so you need to find out what to do first.  There isn't any one telltale sign of poisoning: Symptoms vary with the substance but can include uncommon clumsiness, profuse sweating, paleness, choking, a racing or slowing heartbeat or seizures.  Lesson 5: Supervise children when they're in danger of falling.  Falls account for more than a third of kids' injuries seen in emergency departments. According to Dr. Jean Ogborn, director of the Pediatric Emergency Medical Fellowship Program at the Johns Hopkins University School of Medicine in Baltimore.  Playgrounds are fraught with danger. Choose an area with a surface that has six to 12 inches of soft material, such as rubber matting, sand or mulch, for gentle landings. And check the equipment for excess wear and tear or rust _ which could cause a swing to break apart, for instance.  Lesson 6: Keep your child flat after a serious fall.  If your child falls and ends up with a head injury and appears dazed or unconscious or is bleeding profusely, or if she falls a great distance, such as out of a second-story window, keep her in a flat position and call 911 immediately. Then return to your child.  If she stops breathing or if you're not sure if she's breathing, remove any obvious obstructions from in front of her face, nose or mouth, but don't go fishing into the mouth.  Position her on her back with her head and neck in a straight line and check for breathing by feeling in front of the nose. Then try a rescue breath or two by pinching her nose and breathing into her mouth to see if you can get her chest to rise, Ogburn says.  Lesson 7: Monitor your child after a moderate fall.  With less traumatic falls or head injuries, make sure that your child can comfortably move her arms and legs, and that her pupils aren't dilated (larger than normal). Also watch out for behavioral changes, such as disorientation or persistent crying.  ``It's a fallacy that a child should not be allowed to go to sleep,'' says Dr. Alfred Sacchetti, research director of the department of emergency medicine at Our Lady of Lourdes Medical Center in Camden, N.J. But it is important to wake her up every hour or two to see how she's acting.  Over the next few hours, also keep an eye out for these problems: If she vomits more than twice or has a significant headache, call an ambulance or go to the emergency room.  Lesson 9: Insist that your child wear protective gear for sports  More than 2.5 million kids a year visit ERs because of sports injuries. And we're not just talking about football and baseball. The in-line skating craze has spawned an epidemic of wrist, forearm, elbow, knee and ankle injuries. Bike accidents can cause serious head injuries, as well as lacerations and broken bones.  Protective gear is the key to preventing these injuries. And the onus is on parents to make sure the proper pads, guardsand helmets are worn every time your child plays a sport.  Lesson 10: Go to the ER if a laceration is large or deep.  Cuts that are deep and gaping wide open, bleeding profusely for more than 10 minutes or located on a joint, hand or the face need to be treated quickly in order to stop the bleeding, to prevent infection and to speed healing.  Lesson 11: If a burn appears severe, call 911.  An estimated 350,000 kids under the age of 19 are treated for burn injuries in ERs each year.  With third degree burns (the most serious), blisters often form right away and the skin may appear white, charred or hard. Third-degree burns may not be painful because they often damage nerve endings.  With a second-degree burn, the skin is likely to blister but remain pink according to Dr. Bob Schafermeyer, associate chairman for the department of emergency medicine at Carolinas Medical Center in Charlotte, N.C. And if the skin turns red _ like a sunburn _ and your child feels considerable pain, that's probably a first-degree burn.  Any burn that covers the equivalent of three of your child's palms needs medical attention.  How to treat first-degree burns: If something hot has spilled on your child, strip her clothes off immediately. Run the burn under cold water for 10 to 15 minutes or until the pain subsides.  For small burns, apply a topical antibiotic cream, such as Neosporin or Bacitracin, after 15 to 20 minutes. Never put butter on a burn _ this will only hold the heat in. And resist the urge to kiss it, to reduce the risk of infection.  If the burn blisters, have it checked by a doctor.  Lesson 13: Protect kids from large and small bodies of water.  ``Among babies and toddlers, the most common drownings are in bathtubs and buckets of water,'' explains Dr. Richard Cantor, chairman of pediatric emergency medicine for the American College of Emergency Physicians in Washington, D.C. ``A toddler can drown in four inches of water.''  If a toddler reaches into a bucket of water, he could fall in and be unable to right himself.  To prevent such tragedies, never leave a child unattended in a bathtub or on a toilet. Tubs should be drained after use, and toilet covers should be closed and locked until your child is over 5.  To stay safe at the swimming pool, make sure there's at least a 5-foot-high fence with a self-latching lock around the area, and never leave kids unsupervised.  Pre-ER treatment: If, after being submerged in water, your child is breathing hard, has a change in color or is coughing, call 911. If your child is not breathing or if her heart stops, call 911 and initiate CPR. -0- SIDEBAR: ARE YOU PREPARED FOR AN EMERGENCY?  When an accident happens, you don't want to rack your brain for phone numbers or other crucial information. Here's how to gauge if you're well prepared:  _ Do you have the local poison control number, the emergency access number (if it's not 911) and your pediatrician's number posted by the phone?  _ Do you know where the nearest emergency room is _ and the fastest route to get there? Which hospital in your area has the best trauma or pediatric center?  _ Is your child's immunization record up to date and in a place where you can quickly grab it?  _ Do you know which medications your child is allergic to?  _ Does your child wear a Medic Alert bracelet to inform caregivers of medical problems such as diabetes, medication allergies or heart problems?  _ Have you been trained in CPR or basic first aid techniques? If not, contact your local chapter of the American Red Cross or the American Heart Association.  _ Do you have a first aid kit at home? It should contain Ace bandages, antibiotic cream. Band-Aids, adhesive tape, gauze, tweezers, scissors with rounded tips, acetaminophen, instant-activating ice bags, a first aid manual and ipecac syrup.  (Stacey Colino is a San Francisco-based free-lance health writer.)  -------------------------------------------------------------  (To publish this ``separate buy'' article it must be purchased -- the rate is not prohibitive -- from one of these New York Times Syndicate sales representatives:  (--Europe and Asia: KARL HORWITZ in Paris at 47-42-17-11; fax, 47-42-80-44 or 47-42-18-81; telex, 282-942. (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448, or by fax her at 816-822-1444.) Includes a color slide of Glenn Close in a scene from ``101 Dalmatians'' By CINDY PEARLMAN World rights except Chicago (Distributed by New York Times Special Features)  First it was bunnies, now it's puppies. Is anyone in the animal kingdom safe from Glenn Close?  Actually, the woman who killed a little girl's pet rabbit in ``Fatal Attraction'' (1987) and goes after two kids' cute puppies in ``101 Dalmatians,'' opening Nov. 27, says that she hates that image.  ``I feel absolutely terrible, like I'm some monster,'' Glenn Close says. ``I completely adore dogs!''  The actress looks nothing like her cartoonish Cruella DeVil character in an interview at New York's Rihga Royal Hotel. Dressed in a black Armani pants suit with boyish, close-cropped blonde hair, she is, in fact, quite charming.  Unless you're a puppy, perhaps. As ``Dalmatians'' co-star Joely Richardson reports, the film's many dogs didn't take to Close.  ``The first time Glenn came onto the set, Perdy (the lead female Dalmatian) took one look at her and went running off,'' Richardson says in a separate interview. ``Perdy either smelled her cats or didn't like the bold colors Glenn was wearing. Finally on the fourth take, she stayed put. She realized Glenn wasn't going to hurt her.''  And in the hotel today, a group of the film's dogs, brought in for photo opportunities, cower when she approaches them, cooing in a friendly tone.  Old memories die hard even in dogs, perhaps. As Cruella, Close is PETA's worst nightmare come to life, a fur-obsessed maniac with a demonic cackle. She loves fur _ coats, pajamas, sheets ... even a live skunk, which serves as her handbag. Throw in the talon-like fingernails and black-and-white costuming that extends even to her hair, and no one is likely to mistake her for Mary Poppins.  ``My own 8-year-old daughter, Annie, was very scared when she saw me dressed up,'' Close says. ``She kept saying, `Mom, Cruella makes me mad. Can't you be Anita?'''  But Close wanted to be the Mean Queen. In fact, reading John Hughes' initial script, she found the character too bland.  ``I didn't think Cruella was evil enough,'' Close says. ``So we went back to the original cartoon and lifted a lot of lines, because she was so deliciously bad.  ``She would say, `Bash those pups in the head! Do you have any chloroform?''' The actress laughs. ``That was right from the cartoon.''  When trying to find the character, Close says, ``I decided that she was the devil. Her food is black. She likes to be hot, which is why she likes fur. Her sheets are ermine _ the hotter the better, which is what hell is like.''  It was hell for the actress, too.  ``I was in deep pain most of the time,'' she says. ``Cruella's shoes were 4-1/2-inch heels,'' she explains, and the costume was heavy and stiflingly hot _ and too intricate to be removed or even loosened between takes. Then there were the stunts, including an immersion, fur coat and all, in a vat of molasses.  All in all, Close says, the shoot was ``a certain form of torture.''  The role is only the latest evolution in a career which started with sweetness-and-light roles in ``The World According to Garp'' (1982), ``The Natural'' (1983) and ``The Big Chill'' (1984), only to veer into villainy with ``Fatal Attraction,'' ``Dangerous Liaisons'' (1988) and, most recently, a smash Broadway run as the demonic Norma Desmond in ``Sunset Boulevard'' (1995).  ``Cruella,'' Close says, ``is like the cherry on the cake of evil.''  She even takes her bad rep home with her. Mounted in Close's kitchen is the huge knife she wielded in ``Fatal Attraction.''  ``It's only the cardboard knife,'' she hastens to add. ``In the close-ups, when I'm near Michael Douglas' face, they wouldn't allow me to have a real knife.  ``People always come in and say, `Is this what I think it is?'''  The actress laughs. ``I go, `Yeah!' From the start, they know not to mess with me.''  Despite her legendary status _ back in 1987, the National Enquirer tagged her, ``The Most Hated Woman in America'' _ Close isn't the grand dame that one might expect.  ``I expected her to be more of the diva, but she surprised me,'' Richardson says. ``She's very quiet. Frankly, at the end of this shoot I still wasn't sure what Glenn Close was really like.''  Close guards her privacy ... well, closely. She rarely speaks about growing up on a 500-acre Connecticut spread, or her years spent at a boarding school in Switzerland while her father practiced medicine in Zaire.  She won't even discuss her five-year singing stint with the ever-so-cheerful touring company, Up With People.  ``I don't like talking about it,'' she says simply.  Instead, she focuses herself on her work.  ``I always thought acting was a terrific way of expressing myself,'' she says. ``You see, I was painfully shy as a child. But strangely enough, a stage is where I can get out of myself.''  Two Tony Awards, three Emmy nominations and five Oscar nominations into her career, the actress lives quietly in Connecticut with her daughter Annie. Close is currently engaged to Steve Beers, after previous marriages to musician Cabot Wade and venture capitalist James Marlas. Annie's father is producer John Starcke.  Close met Beers while she was performing in ``Sunset Boulevard,'' on which he worked as a production carpenter.  ``I hadn't had a man in my life for three years,'' she recalls with a blush. ``I didn't have anyone to go to (the customary) opening-night (party) with, which is pretty pathetic.  ``I heard Steve was a great guy, so I got up the courage to say, `Can I ask you something?' He said, `Sure' _ and then some big crisis happened on the stage.''  By the time Beers returned from that crisis, Close was in full Norma get-up, and feeling not at all attractive.  ``It was this white Kabuki makeup,'' she recalls. ``He came up and said, `Is now a good time for your question?'  ``My heart sunk. But I still said, `I was wondering, um, if, ah, you would consider going out to dinner sometime.'  ``He paused and looked at me and said, `In a heartbeat,''' she says. ``It was the greatest.''  Of course, Close has heard the talk about the movie star dating a carpenter, but she disregards it.  ``It's not about someone's job. It's about their aura,'' she says.  If her career arc has taken her from goodie-goodies to villains, the next steps will take her to the White House, as she plays first ladies in two upcoming films.  In Tim Burton's ``Mars Attacks,'' her president/husband is Jack Nicholson, while in 1997's ``Air Force One'' she will play opposite Harrison Ford.  ``Something happens on Air Force One and as the First Lady I might have to declare myself president,'' Close says. ``I might be the first woman in charge.''  In her own life, however, Close is clearly already in charge, and her family is her first priority.  ``It's harder and harder for me to leave home,'' she says. ``The memory of childhood is something that's totally in my mind. It will be in my daughter's mind forever, and I want to be a big part of it.''  And incidentally, that house in Connecticut does in fact include two cats, three mice, a tank full of fish, a pony and a horse. No rabbits, but there are three dogs.  So she isn't really Cruella DeVil. Does she even wear fur?  ``I do have a coat that has a fur lining,'' she admits. ``Once I had a fur coat, but I didn't feel comfortable in it. You see, I really do love animals.''  If only those puppies would believe her. c.1996 Cindy Pearlman  (Cindy Pearlman is a Chicago-based free-lance writer.) (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448, or by fax her at 816-822-1444.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) Includes a color slide of Lynn Redgrave and Geoffrey Rush in a scene from ``Shine.'' By IAN SPELLING (Distributed by New York Times Special Features)  What do you call a movie star who doesn't make movies?  Call her offbeat. Call her different. Call her Lynn Redgrave.  She's been a star since she lit up ``Georgy Girl'' (1966), validating the heritage of a name which she shares with two other movie stars, the late Sir Michael Redgrave, her father, and her sister, Vanessa Redgrave.  Since then, however, she's been seen more on stage than on screen. But it's clear that she's missed the movies from the excitement with which she raves about her new film, ``Shine.''  ``I am excited,'' she says with gusto. ``This is a small film that everyone seems to know about, seems to want to see and experience.  ``The only other time a film's reception came so out of the blue was 30 years ago, when I did `Georgy Girl' (1966). Lots of exciting things have happened for me on the stage, but this is an absolute rarity in terms of my films.''  Actually, R doing a film at all is an absolute rarity. ``Shine,'' opening around the country throughout December, is her first in six years.  ``That was not for a lack of wanting, but a lack of being offered anything,'' she says with a laugh that drips sarcasm. ``It was a situation aided and abetted by my longstanding disagreement with Universal Pictures.  ``What major studio wants to hire somebody who's quite loudly and publicly decrying studio behavior?''  The actress' battle with Universal dated from 1981, when she was fired from the TV series ``House Calls.'' The studio blamed it on financial conflicts, while Redgrave said it was because she had insisted on breast-feeding her baby on the set.  Fortunately for Redgrave, ``Shine'' is an independent film and director Scott Hicks, wasn't familiar with Redgrave's studio battles. The Australian director's interest was sparked by his seeing her one-woman show, ``Shakespeare for My Father,'' on tour in Houston.  In one scene, Redgrave wore an Australian cattleman's hat and coat _ and, she relates, something clicked in Hicks.  ``My play is about my search for my relationship with my dad,'' she says, ``and in a sense `Shine' is about a man's relationship with his father.  ``There were all these coincidences and parallels that made Scott think of me for this small but wonderful role in `Shine.'''  Covering a three-decade period from the 1950s to the 1980s, ``Shine'' is set in Australia and tells the real-life story of David Helfgott, a respected classical pianist. Helfgott grew up with a demanding father (Armin Mueller-Stahl) who instilled in his son an appreciation of music _ and then smothered him in it and his overbearing love and jealousy.  By his early 20s, Helfgott cracked and withdrew into his own private world. A decade later, the pianist emerged from a mental institution to win self-assurance, love and public recognition of his prodigious talents.  Redgrave, 53, plays Gillian, the vibrant Australian astrologer who impulsively marries Helfgott (Geoffrey Rush). Gillian's every moment on time energizes ``Shine,'' but Redgrave explains that the character didn't come easily.  She had conversed by telephone with the real Gillian, but met her only late in the shooting process. Until then, she struggled to conceive of the character in acting terms.  ``David and the other characters leaped off the page, but because there was so little of Gillian, it was hard at first to make it work,'' she recalls. ``I'd played real characters before. I saw pictures and videos of Gillian, read articles about her and David, but I was having a tough time of it.  ``I wanted to look and sound like the real Gillian, to replicate her distinctive style. Scott saw what was happening and told me to create my own Gillian and not a carbon copy.  ``I found that frustrating at first,'' she adds, ``because he had an actress whose stock-in-trade is submerging herself in a role and converting herself into someone else. He said, `Give me the essence of Gillian.'''  When Redgrave saw the finished film, she finally understood Hicks' point.  ``The movie is about David, and the audience is in his mind,'' she says. ``The essence Scott kept talking about had to come in at the movie's end and convince the audience that Gillian, whom they know nothing about, would say yes to David when he proposed.  ``It was nerve-wracking because, with a small-but-important part, you have to get it right or you can throw the whole thing off-kilter. I got where I had to be when I realized Gillian didn't need this relationship and wasn't coming from a place of need.  ``She had a wonderful life, yet fell in love with this man. She says `Yes' to him with her eyes wide open, knowing full well that to let him down in any way in the future would be almost criminal.  ``It was a wonderful process for me to discover this character.''  Exploring a character is nothing new to the London-born daughter of stage legend Michael Redgrave. She has followed in his footsteps through many stage, film and television roles. But she has endured a hit-and-miss career, compared to her father or even to her controversial sister Vanessa.  Some observers blame Redgrave's career slowdown, at least in part, on residual resentment toward Vanessa, whose pro-Palestinian pronouncements have infuriated many in the industry.  Redgrave agrees, to a point.  ``They think there was one offspring of Michael and Rachel who was sometimes called Lynn and sometimes called Vanessa,'' she says. ``I suppose it's human nature to lump together siblings, even middle-aged siblings who have their own, very different lives and opinions.''  Redgrave turned her Hollywood isolation into her greatest success in the early 1990s. Finding herself spending a lot of time at home in Los Angeles with her family, which includes husband John Clark and their three children, she decided to create a vehicle for herself.  The result was the autobiographical play, ``Shakespeare for My Father.'' It ran for nearly a year on Broadway, and Redgrave has toured with it throughout America and across the globe. She is now performing it in London.  The play was a chance for the actress to work out some of her own longstanding family issues, particularly those involving her famous but often remote father. Every time she takes to the stage in ``Shakespeare,'' Redgrave says, she realizes that, for all the pressure and expectations of being a Redgrave, it's a privilege to come from such a long line of thespians.  ``If you say Redgrave to people who know their theater, it almost spells `Theater,''' she says. ``That's an incredible thing to be a part of.  ``I never set out for the show to be a cathartic experience, but it has been. I only set out to make myself a job, and I never realized how much of it would write itself.  ``It's had an exhilarating effect on me.''  ``Shakespeare'' is due to run in London through mid-December, after which Redgrave will return home. She hopes the already-glowing critical reception for ``Shine'' will help her to more film roles.  ``That would be nice,'' the actress says. ``That would be very nice.  ``You know, my career has been far broader than I ever imagined it would be. I always wanted to play the great roles, not just supporting roles, and I didn't want to be just the funny clown.  ``I've managed that to a large extent,'' she says. ``One can't do everything, or do exactly what they want all the time, but I've played a lot of the great roles I wanted to play.  ``And I go on having a very diverse, satisfying career.'' c.1996 Ian Spelling  (Ian Spelling is a New York-based free-lance writer.) Includes a 100-word sidebar By SUSAN GOODMAN From Redbook c.1996 Susan Goodman World rights except in Latin America and Singapore (Distributed by New York Times Syndicate.)  Your brain may be your most important sexual organ, but there's no getting around the fact that lovemaking gets better when the rest of your body is in shape. This isn't about looking like a centerfold; it's about intensifying your arousal and pleasure.  Recent studies show that working out can increase your libido, and specific exercises can strengthen and tone those muscles that seem to exist only during sex.  Here are seven steps you can take to get (and stay) sexually fit:  1. Pump it up.  Your heart, that is. A regular aerobic activity, such as cycling, swimming, jogging or stepping on a StairMaster can do wonders for your sex life. These exercises improvecardiovascular endurance, which translates into more staying power in the bedroom.  Aerobic exercise also improves circulation.  ``If you're fit, when you make love blood will flow to the most active muscles _ the arms, legs and genitals _ without compromising other systems,'' explains Rudy Dressendorfer, visiting professor of physical education and sports studies at the University of Alberta.  As a result, your brain still gets plenty of blood, which allows for better distribution of sexual hormones. Increased blood supply also enhances vaginal lubrication.  Aerobic activity may also help put you in the mood for sex. Dr. Linda De Villers, a sex therapist in Santa Monica, Calif., studied more than 8,000 women 18 to 45 years old and found that after beginning a regular exercise program, 40 percent reported increased arousal and 31 percent had sex more often.  The reason: Working out can cause the body to release testosterone, a hormone that increases the sex drive of both men and women, and mood-elevating endorphins. Exercise also increases activity in the left side of the brain, which produces a euphoric effect.  ``This lasts about two hours,'' De Villers says.  2. Strengthen your thighs.  Have you ever tried a new sexual position, only to find that while you are enthusiastic, your thighs are screaming, ``How long do you expect us to keep this up?'' If so, you know how vital it is to have strong muscles.  Aerobic exercises and weight lifting can strengthen these muscles, and yoga can keep them flexible. Lunges help too: Stand between two chairs, clasping your hands firmly on the top of each chair back for support. Lunge back with one leg, then stand up and repeat with the other leg. Start with one set of eight, building up to three sets of 12.  3. Give power to the pelvis  Not only can pelvic strength, like strength in your thighs, allow you to stay with a position longer, but a healthy set of muscles with good circulation is more responsive to pleasure.  The psoas muscle, which rocks the pelvis forward and back and side to side, can be strengthened with yoga.  Kosha Pati, a yoga teacher at Sky Dancing Institute in Mill Valley, Calif., recommends this exercise: Stand with your knees slightly bent. Inhale while gently rocking the pelvis back. Exhale and return to the original position, letting the movement become a smooth, undulating motion. Your weight should remain evenly balanced on your feet.  The abdominal muscles are also crucial to great sex. They help you stay in partially sitting positions without arm support and help keep the lower back strong.  Modified leg lifts help. So do stomach crunches: Lie on your back, knees bent, feet flat on floor and hip width apart. Lifting your head, neck and shoulders, with chin tucked on chest, slowly curl toward your navel. Hold for several seconds, then lower slowly. Repeat, working up to 20 repetitions.  4. Do your Kegels.  If you've had a baby, you've no doubt been told to do Kegel exercises, which strengthen the pubococcygeus (PC) muscles that form the pelvic floor between the legs. Toning these muscles, which contract during orgasm, helps many women climax more easily; yet many of us skip this exercise or do it wrong.  The correct way: Tighten your muscles as if to stop the flow of urine, and hold for three seconds. Relax for three seconds, then repeat, building up your repetitions to 30.  According to Dr. Cynthia Mervis Watson, co-author of ``Love Potions: A Doctor's Guide to Aphrodisia'' (Putnam, 1993), the exercise should be done with legs slightly apart rather than with thighs clamped together. You will find that you are drawing upward with your pelvis. If you find the exercise tiring, it may be that you're also tensing your buttocks and abdomen; try to isolate only your PC muscles.  5. Stretch Yourself.  It's not enough to be strong. You also want to be limber because, let's face it, leg cramps aren't sexy.  Flexibility helps you stay in positions that feel good and move into new ones you've never tried before.  ``Flexibility definitely enhances creative sexual expression,'' says Joanne Rowley, a registered nurse, yoga teacher and psychotherapist in Boston. ``Ease of movement lets you be more spontaneous with your partner.''  The stretches taught in yoga classes create a good base for flexibility.  ``Imagine the positions you want to get in for lovemaking and do them as stretches,'' suggests Joni Yecalsik, a yoga teacher in New York City.  ``Hold the pose, breathing deeply and stretching just a little more with each exhale.''  6. Learn to Relax  There are times in lovemaking when you want your fit, limber muscles to simply go slack and submit to ecstasy. Relaxation is a skill you can learn _ and one worth developing.  ``Relaxation helps you enjoy being touched and lets you be more physically and emotionally responsive,'' says Dr. Judy Seifer, a West Virginia sex therapist and president of the American Association of Sex Educators, Counselors and Therapists.  For strength and stamina you can't beat aerobic exercise. For relaxation, the mind plays a key role.  ``During conventional orgasms, muscles get so charged and tense they need release, and the experience is over,'' Pati says. Yoga can allow you to be relaxed in high states of arousal, which allows you to prolong pleasure.  Pati recommends this simple exercise: Stand and shake out your body for 10 minutes, part by part, to let out tension.  ``Give yourself up to this `shivering,''' he says. ``Make sure to breathe deeply; feel free to make sounds. These movements help get energy flowing throughout the entire body.''  7. Go for It.  Sex, like any physical activity, is a use-it-or-lose-it proposition.  ``To maintain sexual health, you've got to keep the pilot light burning,'' says Dr. Theresa Crenshaw, author of ``The Alchemy of Love and Lust'' (Putnam, 1996). ``That means sexual thoughts followed by sexual activity _ with or without orgasm, with or without a partner _ anywhere from every other day to once a week. Continuity is important; otherwise your hormone levels change and your mood right along with them.''  Kids, career and grocery shopping may crowd your schedule, but lovemaking should be penciled in to your calendar with an indelible marker. Some women do this by hiding the remote control as soon as their kids are asleep; others set their clocks for 6 a.m., so they can start the day smiling.  After all, the whole point of sexual fitness is to reap the erotic rewards. -0- SIDEBAR: AN ORGASM A DAY ...  Just as physical fitness is good for your sex life, sex is a terrific tonic for your health. Lovemaking can:  _ Relieve pain. Studi sex can combat headaches and temporarily ease PMS.  _ Fight heart disease. Regular lovemaking increases a woman's estrogen level, which helps combat atherosclerosis, maintains bone density and keeps the skin and vaginal tissues supple and lubricated.  _ Boost immunity. Sex can increase the number of antibodies in your bloodstream and reduce unhealthy stress.  _ Burn calories. Okay, so it's not the world's best workout, but it sure is the most entertaining way to burn 100 to 250 calories an hour. (--Europe and Asia: KARL HORWITZ in Paris at 47-42-17-11; fax, 47-42-80-44 or 47-42-18-81; telex, 282-942. (--Mexico, Central America, South America: PAUL FINCH in Los Angeles, 310-996-0075; fax, 310-996-0089. Includes a 100-word sidebar. By WILLIAM STEPHEN CROSS c.1996 William Stephen Cross (Distributed by New York Times Syndicate.)  It's 12:30 a.m. on a Friday, an hour when most normal adults are winding down with Letterman or finishing off a nightcap before heading home. I'm asking myself what a 45-year-old family man with a postgraduate degree is doing on the ice with 30 other men and women dressed up in plastic armor and carrying wooden, laminate and aluminum sticks?  Why, waiting for the referee to drop the puck. It hits the ice and it's skate 'til you drop _ two hours of fast-paced rushes, stick-handling and the occasional goal.  Not the fastest one on the ice, I try parking myself beside our opponent's net. Sure enough, a loose puck slides along the ice to my feet, and without pausing to think about it, I shovel it in. Suddenly I no longer feel the late hour or my sore muscles, I've scored my first goal!  I'm not alone in my jubilation. Adult ice hockey has quietly become the fastest growing sport in North America and Europe, especially in the over-35 age group. NUMBERS ON ICE  The loose, informal organization of most teams and leagues means no one has precise figures, but various estimates put the number of adults in hockey leagues at 250,000 and growing by 15 percent each year. These figures are probably far higher if pick-up games, club hockey and other casual contests could be tracked.  Fans of the professional game, who once thought it was too late for them to play because they never played as kids, have discovered that nothing is more electrifying than watching that little piece of vulcanized rubber stretch the twine at the back of the net.  Operators of skating arenas report that the recent boom in construction of ice rinks simply can't keep pace with the demand at the adult and child level. Teams often happen when a group of friends get together and rent some ice time, or get assembled at arenas into ``house leagues'' with seasons and play-offs.  There's even an international league, Hockey North America (HNA) with over 10,000 members of both genders in over 25 cities. HNA also has a sister league in Holland called Ice Hockey Europe, and the sport is flourishing in Germany, Denmark, Sweden, Finland, Switzerland and even northern Italy.  Adult hockey players come from all walks of life. A stockbroker, who identifies himself as ``Andrew'' (lest his boss discover he's spending a long lunch hour playing hockey), admitted to keeping a beeper wrapped in a towel on the bench.  ``I have sell orders on file at the office from all my clients,'' he says, ``so I can protect them in case the market goes into a tailspin while I'm on the ice.''  While it's true that far more men than women play, many teams have at least one female player. And while women are not normally considered physically aggressive, this reporter was dumped quite rudely in front of the net during a game by a female forward. As the referee skated up ice with the play, she deftly pulled my skates out from under me. GETTING STARTED  Anyone interested in playing for the first time will progress faster if they can already skate, but it's certainly not a requirement.  Hockey North America, for example, has a beginner course designed for people who have never gotten any closer to a game than their TV set. It starts with the fundamentals, then moves on to a 10-game season complete with playoffs.  After that, better players and teams can choose from three levels of play. House leagues often accept all comers, rarely making any effort to assign players based on ability.  Season costs average around $400, but vary by location, usually according to the price for ice-rink rental. Regions with fewer rinks tend to be higher than those with more venues.  Playing hockey calls for short bursts of intense activity, followed by periods spent on the bench. Those starting out should get into shape first. In-line skating or bicycling (both the stationary and the outdoor variety) help improve conditioning in the critical leg muscles and heart. It's also a good idea to have a complete check-up with your family physician before entering into any strenuous exercise regimen. REALITY CHECK  While professional hockey has a decided image problem because of fighting, most adult leagues discourage or even ban fisticuffs outright, as well as body checking, the process of separating the puck from the player by ramming him into the side boards.  This doesn't mean there isn't contact, especially among beginner players still mastering the art of stopping. But players who lose their tempers get tossed out of games, and persistent offenders usually get banned.  Perhaps in emulation of their National Hockey League heroes, some players occasionally end up in shoving matches or exchanges of verbal unpleasantries. When the puck's up for grabs around the goal, pileups are common, causing outbursts of temper and frustration. But most players will agree that having to explain a black eye from a hockey fight to a spouse or boss is a rarity.  Like many other players, my own shiny new hockey career began after watching how much fun my 7-year-old son had on the ice. I signed up for an adult instruction course at a local rink, then played pickup games until I felt I was ready. After that, a phone call to a league and an exchange of money got me assigned to the Gamblers.  My initial excitement at ``making the team'' was tempered a bit, though, when I found out they'd gone 0-32 over the past three seasons. But in the dressing room at our first game, all the players were friendly and enthusiastic _ a mood that slipped noticeably after the team absorbed an 11-1 thrashing.  The Gamblers' record of flawless imperfection continued through the next five games. But my goal was never winning a championship, it was to play the game I've always loved watching.  Whatever happens now, I've won a victory of sorts, a victory over aging, my inhibitions and self-doubts. Can Wayne Gretzky claim that? SIDEBAR: THE PUCK STOPS HERE  Getting started:  Almost every rink now has adult ice hockey programs; check the yellow pages for the nearest ice skating rink. The largest single league is Hockey North America. For information, call 800-4-HOCKEY.  Cost: A 20-game season (90 minutes per game) with double elimination playoffs costs around $400, depending on your location (ice time costs vary by region).  What you need: Most programs require (in addition to hockey skates) a regulation helmet, gloves, elbow and shoulder pads, pants and shin guards. Men will want to include an athletic supporter with a cup.  Depending on the brand and quality of the equipment, expect to spend at least $400 to $500 for a complete set; this figure does not include skates ($100-$350) or sticks ($18 for an inexpensive wooden one up to $100 for a graphite model with replaceable blade).  Some stores now feature used equipment, and many rinks have bulletin boards offering equipment for sale. Goalie equipment is much more expensive (pads can run as much as $1,500 new), and is best purchased used.  Visors or full face shields are highly recommended. Don't skimp on your equipment _ even with no checking allowed, collisions and spills demand solid protection. Times News Service  TIMOR BISHOP ACCEPTS NOBEL PEACE PRIZE  OSLO, Norway (NYT) _ Amid reports that he had been threatened by the Indonesian government, Carlos Felipe Ximenes Belo, a Catholic bishop from the occupied territory of East Timor, steered clear of confrontation Tuesday as he accepted the 1996 Nobel Peace Prize.  By contrast the co-winner of the prize, a militant resistance leader who lives in exile in Australia, let loose a cannonade of criticism against Indonesia and put forward a plan for East Timorese independence.  The exile leader, Jose Ramos-Horta, said Indonesian authorities had warned the bishop that `if he was too free-talking or if he was seen too often with me he would suffer the consequences.`  IRAQ PUMPS OIL FOR EXPORT AGAIN  CAIRO, Egypt (NYT) _ Iraq began Tuesday to pump oil abroad for the first time in six years as President Saddam Hussein reopened a 616-mile pipeline that will deliver Iraqi crude to terminals on the Turkish coast.  At a ceremony in the northern oil city of Kirkuk, Hussein proclaimed that Iraq had returned ``to the international market,'' the official Iraqi News Agency reported.  But experts said the first oil to emerge from the pipeline would be suitable only for asphalt. They said it could be weeks before the limited quantity of oil that Iraq will be permitted to sell under a strict United Nations plan reaches the world stage.  GERMANS, CZECHS SET TO APOLOGIZE FOR WARTIME ACTIONS  BONN, Germany (NYT) _ More than half a century after the end of World War II, Germany will apologize for Hitler's invasion of the former Czechoslovakia, and Prague will express regrets for the postwar expulsion of millions of Sudeten Germans, according to the draft of a joint declaration to be endorsed this month.  German officials said Tuesday that Foreign Minister Klaus Kinkel and his Czech counterpart, Josef Zieleniec, would meet in Prague on Dec. 20 to initial the long-negotiated document intended to end a corrosive dispute between the two countries.  The draft document, leaked to reporters in Prague, commits Germany to apologizing for Nazi ``policies of violence'' while the Czech side will express regret that the postwar expulsion of 2.5 million Sudeten Germans caused suffering and injustice to innocent people.  SOUTH AFRICA PANEL GRANTS AMNESTY TO WHITE OFFICER  JOHANNESBURG, South Africa (NYT) _ In a move that signals that the most heinous crimes of apartheid will be forgiven rather than punished, South Africa's Truth and Reconciliation Commission on Tuesday granted amnesty to a white police officer who was already serving a life sentence for his role in the brutal slaying of 11 people.  The police officer, Brian Mitchell, master-minded a 1988 attack that had come to be known as the Trust Feed massacre after the rural community where it took place. The night-time raid was aimed at political activists linked to President Nelson Mandela's African National Congress. But the wrong hut was identified, and the attackers burst in on a group of women and children attending a wake.  For months, South Africans have waited to see how the Commission would handle a high profile case involving what seemed like wanton viciousness by representatives of the old white government. Now they have their answer.  PANEL OKS JEWISH HOUSING IN ARAB EAST JERUSALEM  JERUSALEM (NYT) _ An Israeli planning commission approved construction of the first housing development for Jews in an Arab neighborhood in East Jerusalem on Tuesday, drawing Palestinian protests and warnings of renewed conflict over this bitterly contested city.  The plan to build 132 housing units in the Ras el-Amud neighborhood must be signed by Interior Minister Eliahu Suissa or another authorized minister before work can begin. But Suissa publicly endorsed the scheme Tuesday, saying it would be coupled with approval of more Arab housing in the area.  The plan to house Jews in Ras el-Amud, delayed under the previous Labor party government because of its political sensitivity, has raised concerns that it might dangerously escalate tension between Israelis and Palestinians.  NATO TAKES STEP TO INCLUDE FORMER EAST BLOC NATIONS  BRUSSELS, Belgium (NYT) _ NATO took the first formal step Tuesday to expand its alliance to include formerly Communist nations of Eastern Europe and then moved quickly to ease Russia's apprehensions by offering to negotiate a special charter with Russia and to increase military cooperation and contacts.  The foreign ministers of NATO's 16 member states, meeting at the alliance's headquarters near here, also sought to reassure Russia by pledging that the alliance had ``no intention, no plan and no reason'' to move nuclear weapons in the territory of any new members.  Russia's foreign minister, Yevgeny Primakov, arrived Tuesday evening for talks with his counterparts in NATO, but made no public statements after meeting separately with NATO's secretary general, Javier Solana, and Secretary of State Warren Christopher. Primakov is to appear before the entire NATO membership and other Eastern European nations on Wednesday morning, where officials expect he will address the issue.  CLINTON CHIDES SENATE FOR NOT RATIFYING WOMEN'S RIGHTS TREATY  WASHINGTON (NYT) _ Surrounded by half a dozen women's rights advocates from around the world, President Clinton Tuesday sought to blunt criticism of his administration's record on human rights by chiding the Republican-controlled Senate for failing to ratify a 15-year-old United Nations treaty guaranteeing women's rights.  ``As you know, many, many, many other nations have done this,'' Clinton said at a small ceremony at the White House marking International Human Rights Day, with his wife, Hillary. ``In our country, where we have worked so hard against domestic violence, where we have worked so hard to empower women, it is, to say the least, an embarrassment that the United States has not done this, and there is no excuse for this situation to continue.''  BRITAIN WITHDRAWS FROM U.N. INDUSTRIAL DEVELOPMENT ORGANIZATION  VIENNA (NYT) _ Pressure for reform within the United Nations has taken a somewhat strange twist, with a decision by the British to withdraw from the United Nations Industrial Development Organization, even while acknowledging that the organization deserves high marks for the reforms that it has instituted.  The British ambassador to the United Nations organizations here, Christopher Hulse, said Tuesday that the withdrawal ``was more directed at New York'' than at the organization. But the organization, which has already been targeted by the Clinton administration, may ultimately be the fatal victim, its immediate future hanging on a decision by the German government, which is expected on Wednesday.  ``We are worried about the lack of coordination in the U.N. system, and wastefulness,'' Hulse said. ``That is the message that we want to be firmly established with the new secretary general.'' </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19961210.0471 type=story > <HEADLINE> ROBERTSON STEPHENS'S ROHAL LIKES MSFT, INTC: BLOOMBERG FORUM </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> New York, Dec. 10 (Bloomberg) (BC-ROBERTSON-STEPHENS-FORUM-BLOOM) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_199612.gz"

THANKSGIVING  THANKSGIVING-BUSH (Undated) _ A conversation with Laura Bush. At a time when there are so many uncertainties, one thing is immutable: President Bush and his family will have a Thanksgiving dinner that hardly differs from the past. Very traditional, with a few Texas touches and one new grace note from the White House pastry chef, Roland Mesnier. What isn't certain, or at least isn't being revealed, is where it will be. Camp David? The White House? The family ranch in Texas? However much the Bushes try to make this a Thanksgiving like all the others, it is, of course, so very different. By Stuart Elliott.  COMMENTARY (Moved in ``k'' category)  KRUGMAN-COLUMN _ Commentary by Paul Krugman.  SPORTS (Moved in ``s'' category)  FBN-SEASON (Undated) _ An On Pro Football column looking at some trends around the league as the NFL moves past the halfway point of the season. By Thomas George.  BKW-PREVIEW (Undated) _ Women's college basketball preview, focusing on the relationship between the coaches at Connecticut and Vanderbilt, two schools that are highly ranked for the 2000-2001 season. By Frank Litsky.  ENTERTAINMENT (Moved in ``e'' category)  TERROR-ART (Undated) _ How do artists respond to momentous acts of violence? Some are stunned into silence, others rush forward to express their feelings with a poem or a painting. But many allow for a time of reflection until they can begin to grasp the meaning and approach it within a creative context. In the mix of responses, though, there appear to be marked similarities of theme and emotion that transcend time, cultures and particular disasters. These past works of art and literature point toward the likely shape of cultural offerings inspired by the terrorism of Sept. 11, say several experts who have studied what one of them calls ``the art of aftermath.'' By Mel Gussow.  BOSTON-SYMPHONY-TERROR (Undated) _ The controversy over the Boston Symphony's decision to cancel its planned performances of three choruses from the John Adams opera ``The Death of Klinghoffer.'' The opera is about the 1985 hijacking of the cruise ship Achille Lauro and the brutal murder of a wheelchair-bound passenger by Palestinian terrorists, but also more broadly about the historical tensions in the Middle East. By Allan Kozinn.  CANADA-LIBERATURE (Undated) _ In recent years Canadian literary critics have been asking why so many Canadian fiction writers confine themselves to provincial, even small-town themes. The answer may lie in the rewards. Last week Canada's biggest literary prize went to ``Clara Callan,'' a slow-moving, low-key novel couched mostly in the form of letters between two sisters from small-town Ontario traveling sharply divergent paths to maturity. By Barbara Crossette.  BABEL-BOOK-REVIEW (Undated) _ ``The Complete Works of Isaac Babel,'' edited by Babel's daughter, Nathalie, and marvelously translated by Peter Constantine, is a cause for a kind of melancholy celebration _ a celebration of literary genius framed by 20th century tragedy. Babel, an Odessa Jew who was executed in 1940 at the age of forty-six, is probably not among the very greatest writers of the 20th century, but he comes close. His stories and sketches, his journalism and diaries, even his screenplays have an electrifying cumulative impact. Reviewed by Richard Bernstein.  TV-BERNIE-REVIEW (Undated) _ On Fox, ``The Bernie Mac Show.'' Reviewed by Neil Genzlinger.  DINING (Moved in `d' category)  THANKSGIVING-BUSH (Undated) _ A conversation with Laura Bush. At a time when there are so many uncertainties, one thing is immutable: President Bush and his family will have a Thanksgiving dinner that hardly differs from the past. Very traditional, with a few Texas touches and one new grace note from the White House pastry chef, Roland Mesnier. What isn't certain, or at least isn't being revealed, is where it will be. Camp David? The White House? The family ranch in Texas? However much the Bushes try to make this a Thanksgiving like all the others, it is, of course, so very different. For them, as for most Americans, this holiday will have more than the usual poignancy and significance. by Marian Burros.  With recipes.  THANKSGIVING-CORN (Undated) _ The dried corn that John Cope's Food Products has been processing and selling for 101 years has always seemed a perfect thing to eat on Thanksgiving. More than anything except the turkey, it's a link to our forefathers, something we all seem to crave. By R.W. Apple Jr.  With recipe.  THANKSGIVING-CIDER (Undated) _ A sparkling solution to the old problem of what to drink with the bird, when neither red nor white wine seems quite right. By Amanda Hesser.
