ENTERTAINMENT &AMP; ARTS  (Coded `e')  ON THE COVER  MUSIC-ALICIA-KEYS (New York) _ Alicia Keys, who turns 21 on Feb. 1, has been nominated for Grammys in six categories, including best new artist and song of the year for ``Fallin'.'' In an interview, she discusses her life, her songs and her plans. By Jon Pareles. (2,000 words)  With photo.  Also see MUSIC-PUBLISHER-GERSON  THEATER  THEATER-POWELL (New York) _ Decades after Dawn Powell was buried anonymously in New York City's potter's field, she is, as Gore Vidal predicted, ``on the verge of ceasing to be a cult and becoming a major religion.'' Dozens of writers and performers will participate in ``Permanent Visitor: A Festival Celebrating Dawn Powell in New York'' through March 9. The event includes performances of five plays, a play about her, stage adaptations of her short stories and performers reading from her diaries and novels _ not the touching ones about Ohio, where she was born in 1897, but the witty ones about New York, where she lived for almost half a century. She died in 1965 with virtually all her 15 novels out of print. By Jonathan Mandell. (1,700 words)  With photo.  THEATER-OLDIES (New York) _ George M. Cohan wrote the line ``give my regards to old Broadway.'' If he were writing it today, the emphasis would be on the ``old.'' This year's Broadway season includes one-woman shows by Beatrice Arthur, 78, Elaine Stritch, 76, and Barbara Cook, 74; new plays by Edward Albee, 73 and Neil Simon, 74; a star turn by Uta Hagen, 82, in a new play; a revival of ``Into the Woods'' by Stephen Sondheim, 71; and two revivals of plays by Arthur Miller, 86. ``We're going through a geriatric phase now,'' said Rocco Landesman, the president of Jujamcyn Theaters. By Mervyn Rothstein. (2,000 words)  With photo.  FILM  FILM-MORETTI (New York) _ Nanni Moretti writes, directs, produces and stars in his movies, using his own preoccupations and opinions as the starting point for his deadpan humor. Moretti's reputation as a satirist explains the surprise that greeted his latest film, ``La Stanza del Figlio'' (``The Son's Room'') when it came out in Europe last year. The film was praised for its austere emotional power both in Italy and in France, where it won the Palme d'Or, at the Cannes International Film Festival. The movie is Italy's submission for this year's foreign film dd Oscar. ``The Son's Room,'' which opens in New York on Feb. 1, is about a close and loving family hit by an unexpected tragedy. (2000 words)  With photo.  TELEVISION  TV-SMALLVILLE (Undated) _ ``Smallville'' is one of a handful of new shows this season to have attained breakout status. While the show isn't quite as glossy as ABC's ``Alias'' and hasn't revolutionized the look of television drama the way Fox's ``24'' has, ``Smallville'' peddles its own brand of American corn, which, when served with a pinch of teenage angst, a hint of paranormality and a fresh take on superman, one of the most durable icons in pop culture history, makes an immensely satisfying meal. By Hal Hinson (2,000 words)  With photo.  TV-BLUE-PLANET (Undated) _ Since the advent of Discovery and its spin-offs, television has become rife with nature and it is much harder to recreate the sense of wonder viewers must have felt when Jacques Cousteau began filming underwater in the 1950s. ``Blue Planet'' contains echoes of those days. The first four hourlong installments of the ``Blue Planet'' will be shown Jan. 27 and Jan. 28 on Discovery. By Julie Salamon. (1,200 words)  MUSIC  APPRECIATION-PEGGY-LEE (Undated) _ Peggy Lee taught Terry Teachout about sex. He was 12 at the time and working his way through his father's record collection that included Peggy Lee's recording of ``Fever.'' ``I got the message, and then some,'' writes Teachout. ``What the lady on the record had in mind was pretty much what I had in mind 24 hours a day, except that her point of view was more informed.'' Thirty-four years later Teachout says he now knows that ``Fever'' was the least of her. ``She was exquisite _ there is no other word for her,'' he says. By Terry Teachout. (480 words)  (See photos NYT2, NYT3 and NYT4 sent on Tuesday with the Peggy Lee obituary.)  MUSIC-DALBAVIE (Paris) _ Marc Andre Dalbavie's new ``Color,'' to be performed by Christoph Eschenbach and the Orchestre de Paris on Jan. 30 at Carnegie Hall, is a little different for him: it's a departure into normality, with the full ensemble on stage, not scattered around a concert hall. By Paul Griffiths. (1,600 words).  MUSIC-PUBLISHER-GERSON (Los Angeles) _ Unlike record producers who help make and market albums or managers who can mold an artist's image, music publishers rarely share in the credit for an artist's success. That is true, in part, because many do little beyond collecting a fee for publishing a song. But the best music publishers not only aggressively promote their artists' work but also work closely with artists' managers to nurture talent. That's the kind of publisher Jody Gerson has been for Alicia Keys. By Laura M. Holson. (1,000 words)  ART  EXHIBIT-WOMEN-CHINA-PAINTERS (New York) _ Women ruled the worlds of china painting beginning in the 1870s, when they organized in groups to do china painting. Their influence increased in the 1880s as they founded factories, which employed mostly female artists. By then, women with increased leisure time began painting china at home, responding to advertisements urging them to buy their own blanks, brushes, paints and kilns. By the 1890s, china painting was a do-it-yourself craze that women across America joined for pleasure or profit. Now the role of women in this movement is recognized with ``Women China Decorators in the Metropolitan Museum of Art,'' on view through April 14. By Rita Reif. (1,100 words)  With photo.  DANCE  DANCE-JONES (New York) _ As he nears 50, Bill T. Jones has shed a degree of his political vehemence and is searching for beauty and for a new home for the Bill T. Jones/Arnie Zane Dance Co. The quest for beauty has produced three new works to be performed at Alice Tully Hall beginning on Jan. 31. The search for a home is for more than a building; it is to ensure the future of his choreography and his company. By Wendy Perron. (1,800 words)  BESTSELLERS, CHILDREN'S-BESTSELLERS and PAPERBACKS moved on Monday, Jan. 21.  TRAVEL  (Coded `t')  PRACTICAL-TRAVELER (Undated) _ Companies specializing in travel for singles say they add a lot of value. Matching roommates helps travelers cut costs by avoiding charges for single rooms and the absence of couples and families keeps singles from feeling like a fifth wheel. By Hope Reeves. (1,200 words)  TRAVEL-SECURITY-AGENCY (Undated) _ In the next few weeks, travelers will begin noticing that the emblems on security screeners' uniforms will change to the Transportation Security Administration. Behind the scenes, changes will be even bigger as the agency adds 31,000 employees and converts 25 airports per week. By Matthew L. Wald (1,900 words)  CUBA-CRUISES (Undated) _ Much of the U.S. travel industry has long been eyeing Cuba as a destination, and no segment more covetously than cruise lines. Sept. 11 has made Cuba even more inviting as cruise lines added more Caribbean itineraries. Projections show show that if the ban on travel by Americans to Cuba were lifted, 750,000 American cruise passengers a year would visit Cuba in the first few years and the numbers would grow to 1.8 million in a decade, more if ferry service could be established. By Edwin McDowell. (850 words)  TRAVEL-MEXICO-QUERETARO (Undated) _ Queretaro's plazas and chapels were settings for the rise and fall of colonial power in Mexico. From here Franciscan missionaries launched campaigns in the early 16th century to force Indians into slavery and Christianity. Ignacio Perez, Mexico's Paul Revere, rode out from this city in 1810 to warn the organizers of the War of Independence of a plot to kill them. The last Mexican emperor, Archduke Maximilian, was executed here in 1867 and in 1917 in the Teatro de la Republica, Mexico's Constitution was drafted. ``If you want to walk in history,'' one guide said, ``then this is a city that has worked hard to preserve it.'' By Ginger Thompson. (1,500 words)  SHORT STORIES  TRAVEL-Q&AMP;A (Undated) _ Answers to questions about getting married on a cruise from Venice to Rome, tours associated with J.S. Bach, comment cards in hotels. By Pamela Noel (1,100 words)  TRAVEL-BRIEFS (Undated) _ Four Items: England declares it is free of foot and mouth disease; New Orleans adds extra police for Mardi Gras; La Scala is closed until 2004; United and Northwest Airlines share E-tickets, and Legoland California cuts hours. By Frances Frank Marcus, Marina Harss and Marjorie Connelly. (900 words)  TRAVEL-NY-RITZ-CARLTON (New York) _ On Sept. 11, the luxury Ritz-Carlton chain was less than a month from opening a hotel in Battery Park City, five blocks south of the World Trade Center. The opening was delayed until Jan. 29. About two-thirds of the 298-rooms, face the water, and each of these has a telescope to take in the sights, most notably the Statue of Liberty. By Ray Cormier. (Rina Castelnuovo/The New York Times)  (NYT29) SNOHOMISH, Wash. -- Oct. 18, 2006 -- HOME-REPAIR-CLUBS-5 -- Members from a home repair club at a lunch break at a communal contracting party held at the home of Karen and Keith Reidel in Snohomish, Wash., in September 2006. Nearly a quarter-century ago, three couples, all friends, began working on one another's homes in Monroe, 33 miles north of Seattle and a few minutes from Snohomish. It was a decision born of necessity. None of us had a lot of money, and this was a way to get work done, said one of them, Sam Lockwood. Years later, Lockwood and his friends are still working. They have installed bathtubs, landscaped yards and built boat docks. From three couples they have grown to seven, including the adult son of one of the couples and his wife. The group is one of the more enduring examples of home repair clubs that have emerged across the country, connecting friends and neighbors while saving on the rising costs of professional renovations. Naslund bought a house she had long coveted in Louisville after her 1998 novel Ahab's Wife became a best seller. Those pondering how to nurture the muse might want to check out the Naslund's mansion. Naslund, who is 64, wrote her first book for a no-figure advance, her second for the same, and the next two for not much more. Receiving a royalty check for $350, she sent it back to the publisher, suggesting, without irony, that it would be better used for advertising. Then, in her mid 50s, she wised up and got an agent, followed by a $410,000 advance for what would be the critically acclaimed best seller, Ahab's Wife. (David R.
